Commentary
This passage presents the proof of God's omniscience and responds to a series of objections. The proof is a syllogism: God's acts are precise and perfect (premise 1, sensory), and anything with precise acts is all-knowing (premise 2, self-evident), therefore God is all-knowing. Then objections arise: First, the world might be the act of an intermediary. The author grants this but then analyzes what 'precise act' means—either beneficial in every respect (rejected, as the world has afflictions), beneficial in some respects (granted but insufficient, as inattentive beings can produce occasional benefit), or conventionally good (either absolutely best—unknown if world is so—or generally good—granted but insufficient). Thus the definition of 'precise act' must be clarified. Second objection: possibility (makāna) is not present at the moment of acquisition because the acquired is necessary. The answer: what is acquired is the ability to acquire in the future, which is compatible with present possibility. Third objection: the powerful is said to be hesitant between act and omission, but omission is not an object of power. Answer: the meaning is that he can act or not act, not that he makes omission an object. Fourth objection (of the second type): the ability to have an effect in pre-eternity is contradictory; rather, absolute ability demands the validity of the effect afterwards. Fifth objection: the empowered must be distinct; answer: intellectual distinction suffices. Sixth objection: correlation with an absolute empowered is unclear; with a specific empowered it is a relation called creatorhood. Last objection: causing-to-exist is an attribute; if possible, division recurs; if necessary, the thing is necessary. Answer: it is a relational attribute (see earlier discussion). Thus the proof stands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power requires a real, pre-existing distinctness in the thing created.
Correction: Al-Razi says intellectual distinction (tamyīz 'aqlī) is sufficient for the thing to be the object of power.

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's answer to the objection that the empowered must be distinct from others?
A) Physical distinction is required.
B) Intellectual distinction suffices.
C) The objection is invalid.
D) Distinction is not needed.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi concludes that the objections to God's omniscience and power are resolved by careful attention to the meanings of possibility, power, and relational attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for a specific need, do you sometimes doubt that God can bring it about because you cannot see how it could be distinct from other possibilities? How does al-Razi's point that intellectual distinction suffices challenge that doubt and call you to deeper trust in God's knowledge and power?
?
If causing-to-exist is merely a relational attribute of God, not an essential one, does that make God seem distant or impersonal to you? Reflect on whether your heart demands that God's power be 'essential' to feel close, and ask yourself: Is your faith in God's attributes based on how they make you feel, or on what He has revealed?
?
Which of the three objections in this passage resonates most with your own inner struggles about God's ability to act in the world—the need for distinctness, the confusion about how God relates to specific things, or the worry that God's causing-to-exist is either limited or forced? Repent of any attempt to confine God to human logic, and commit to accepting His descriptions of Himself as relational and transcendent.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The Proof of God's Knowledge from Well-Ordered Acts
Razi states the consensus, the syllogistic proof, and the objection regarding the intermediary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogistic proof that God is knowing based on the order in creation, and how Imam Razi clarifies the meaning of 'well-ordered act'.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is proven by the evident order in His creation.

Imam Razi presents a straightforward proof: the world exhibits clear order and precision, and such order can only come from a knowing agent. This establishes God's knowledge. However, an objector may challenge that the world might not be directly God's act, or that 'well-ordered' is ambiguous. Razi responds by enumerating possible meanings of 'well-ordered act' to ground the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the three components
Read the passage and identify the claim, the syllogism (two premises), and the objection regarding the intermediary.
2
Understand the two premises
Note that the first premise (God's acts are well-ordered) is sensory, and the second (everything with well-ordered acts is knowing) is self-evident.
The author considers these intuitive.
3
Examine the objection
The objector doubts the world is directly from God; Razi concedes but shifts to defining 'well-ordered'.
This shift is key to the argument.
4
Learn the three definitions
Imam Razi provides three possible meanings of 'well-ordered act': benefit, custom, or some other meaning.
This sets up the next lesson's analysis.

مسألة: اتفق جمهور العقلاء على أنه تعالى عالم إلا قدماء الفلاسفة لها أفعاله محكمة متقنة فكل ما كان كذلك فهو عالم ، والمقدمة الأولى حسية ، والثانية بديهية ، فإن قبل لا نعلم أن هذا العالم فعله ، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون فعل الواسطة ، سلمناه لكن المراد من الفعل المحكم هو الذي يكون مطابقا للمنفعة أو ما يكون مستحسنا في العرف أو أمر ثالثا ، فإن أردتم به الأول فإما أن تريدوا به كون الفعل مطابقا للمنفعة من كل الوجوه أو من بعض الوجوه ، فإن أردت به الأول فهو ممنوع ولم قلتم إن المخلوقات مطابقة المنفعة من كل الوجوه فظاهر أنها ليست كذلك لكثرة ما نشاهد في العالم من الآفات ، إن أردت به الثاني فمسلم لكن كون الفعل مشتملا على النفع من بعض الوجوه لا يدل على كون فاعله عالما ، لأن فعل الساهي والنائم بل الحركات الصادرة عن الجمادات قد يكون نافعة من بعض الوجوه ، وأما إن أردتم بالمحكم ما يكون مستحسنا في العرف فإما أن تريدوا [؟] ما يكون مستحسنا على وجه لا يمكن [؟] هو أحسن منه أو تريدوا به كونه مستحسنا في الجملة ، فإن أردت به الأول فلا نعلم أن العالم كذلك فإنا لا ندرى أن ترتيب الكواكب في السموات أو ترتيب أبدان الحيوانات على وجه أكمل فيهما و [؟] الآن عليه ممكن أم لا ، فإن أردت به الثاني فمسلم أن العالم كذلك لكنه لا يدل على الفاعل، فإن فعل الساهي والنائم قد يستحسن من بعض الوجوه ، فأما إن أردت بالإحكام والإتقان معنى ثالثا فاذكروه لنتكلم عليه
Issue: The majority of rational people agree that He, exalted, is knowing except the ancient philosophers. His acts are well-ordered and precise, and everything that is like that is knowing. The first premise is sensory, and the second is self-evident. If it is said: We do not know that this world is His act, and it is not possible that it be the act of an intermediary, we concede that, but what is meant by 'well-ordered act' is that which corresponds to benefit, or that which is deemed good in custom, or a third matter. If you take the first, then you either mean that the act corresponds to benefit in all respects or in some respects. If you take the first, it is denied. Why do you say that created things correspond to benefit in all respects? It is apparent that they are not like that due to the many harms we observe in the world. If you take the second, it is conceded, but that the act contains some benefit does not indicate that its doer is knowing, because the act of the forgetful and the sleeping, indeed the movements emanating from inanimate objects, may be beneficial in some respects. As for if you take 'well-ordered' to mean that which is deemed good in custom, then you either mean something deemed good in such a way that it is not possible for something better to exist, or you mean it is deemed good in general. If you take the first, we do not know that the world is like that, for we do not know whether the arrangement of the planets in the heavens or the arrangement of animal bodies is in the most complete manner possible or not. If you take the second, it is conceded that the world is like that, but it does not indicate the doer, for the act of the forgetful and the sleeping may be deemed good in some respects. As for if you mean by 'well-ordered and precise' a third meaning, then mention it so we can discuss it.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This is a definition and proof passage. The author, Imam Razi, begins by stating the issue: the majority of rational people agree that God is knowing, except the ancient Greek philosophers. He then presents a syllogistic proof: God's acts are well-ordered and precise (muḥkama mutqana), and every being whose acts are well-ordered is knowing. The first premise is sensory (we observe order in the world), the second is self-evident (we intuitively link order to knowledge). An objector says we do not know if the world is God's direct act; it could be through an intermediary. The author concedes this but shifts to defining 'well-ordered act' (al-fiʿl al-muḥkam). He enumerates three possible definitions: 1) corresponding to benefit (manfaʿa), 2) deemed good in custom (mustaḥsan fī al-ʿurf), or 3) some other meaning. He then refines each: if benefit, one must distinguish between absolute benefit (which is not present due to harms) and partial benefit (which is insufficient to prove knowledge, as even the forgetful or inanimate things can produce partial benefit). If custom, one must distinguish between maximal perfection (which we cannot verify) and general goodness (which again does not prove knowledge). He concludes by challenging the objector to propose a third definition. The aim is to clear the ground for the proof.

Key Terms
مُحْكَم
مُتْقَن
فَاعِل
مَنْفَعَة
مُسْتَحْسَن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of God's knowledge fails if the world is not directly created by Him.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that even if the world is through an intermediary, the proof can be salvaged by clarifying what 'well-ordered' means.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise of the proof for God's knowledge?
A) God's acts are well-ordered.
B) Everything that has well-ordered acts is knowing.
C) The world could not be without a cause.
D) The ancient philosophers denied knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi establishes that God's knowledge is proven by the evident order in creation, and clarifies the key term to prevent confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the order in your own life—your health, your provisions, your circumstances—do you genuinely see them as evidence of God's knowledge, or do you take them for granted? How would your trust in Him change if you truly believed every detail was perfectly arranged by an all-knowing God?
?
The objection in the source suggests that blessings might come through intermediaries. Have you ever thanked a person or circumstance while forgetting that God is the ultimate cause? What would it look like to shift your gratitude entirely to Him today, even for the smallest benefit?
?
Razi argues that the connection between order and knowledge is self-evident. Yet, how often do you live as if your own life is random or meaningless? If you truly accept that God's knowledge is proven by order, what comfort or demand does that place on you when facing uncertainty or suffering?

Analyzing the Meanings of 'Muhkam': Benefit and Custom
Razi refutes the definitions of 'muhkam' as absolute benefit and maximal custom, showing they are either false or insufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definitions of 'well-ordered' as absolute benefit or maximal custom are rejected, and why partial benefit or general custom do not prove a knowing agent.

Core Idea
Neither utility nor custom alone can serve as a reliable indicator of a knowing creator.

In this continuation, Imam Razi systematically examines the two definitions of 'well-ordered act' that were introduced: benefit and custom. He shows that if taken in an absolute sense, they are not empirically true; if taken in a partial sense, they are insufficient to prove knowledge, because even random or mindless actions can be partially beneficial or acceptable. He then challenges any proponent of a third definition to come forward.

Guided Path
1
Read and note the two definitions under analysis
The passage examines the first definition (benefit) and the second (custom). Identify the two branches for each.
2
Understand the refutation of benefit
Absolute benefit is denied due to harms; partial benefit is conceded but insufficient, as even inanimate actions can be partially beneficial.
3
Understand the refutation of custom
Maximal custom is unknown; general custom is conceded but insufficient, as random acts can be generally acceptable.
4
Recognize the challenge for a third definition
Razi calls for a third meaning to be proposed, showing the argument remains open.

?

?

?

Are you comfortable with that uncertainty, or do you feel a need to prove that everything is perfectly ordered? How does admitting ignorance about the optimality of creation affect your trust in God's wisdom?

The Second Objection on Capability and Its Refutation
Razi states and refutes the second objection on capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the proof from well-orderedness, and how Imam Razi refutes it by distinguishing present capability from future acts.

Core Idea
Present capability is for future acts, not for acts already occurring.

In the ongoing dialectic, an opponent raises the issue of capability (al-makina). The objection claims that if an act is already occurring, capability for it cannot exist because actuality and possibility are incompatible. Imam Razi’s response clarifies the temporal scope of capability. The following passage presents this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the second objection which claims that capability (al-makina) is not established in the state of occurrence because the actual is necessary and its opposite is impossible.
2
Understand the initial answer
The answer states that what is obtained in the present is the ability to obtain in the future, not the present act itself.
3
Note the refinement regarding the servant's power
This answer does not apply to the power of the servant if power is concomitant with the act; the present capability is for future acts.
4
Grasp the logical conclusion
Future occurrence can coexist with present capability, but present occurrence cannot. Otherwise, the impossible follows.
This distinguishes divine power from human power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولئن نزلنا عن الاستفسار فلم قلت إن فعل المحكم يدل على علم الفاعل وبيانه من وجوه .
والمعارضة الثانية بأن المنكنة لا تثبت في حال الحصول لأن الحاصل حينئذ واجب ومقابله ممتنع في الحال مدفوعة لما ذكره ، وهو أن الحاصل في الحال هو التمكن من التحصيل فى الاستقبال ، إلا أن ذلك لا يتمشى في قدرة العبد مع القول بكونها مقارنة للفعل .
والتحقيق فيه أن الوقوع في الاستقبال ممكن الإجتماع مع وجود المكنة في الحال وممتنع الاجتماع مع الوقوع في الحال حتى لزم منه المحال . والمعارضة الثالثة : بأن القادر على قولكم متردد بين الفعل والترك ، والترك لا يكون مقدوراً فجوابها أن منه أن يفعل وأن لا يفعل لا أن يفعل الترك ، والمصنف أورد في جوابه ما أورده في
القادر هو الذى يصح جواب المعارضة الثانية ولكن بعبارة أخرى .
وأما ما أورده في النوع الثاني من المعارضة وهو أن التمكن من الأثر يستدعى صحة الأثر ، فالجواب عنه
أن المتمكن من التأثير فى الأزل متناقض ولذلك كان المتمكن من التأثير مطلقا مستدعيا لصحة الأثر بعد ذلك
·
والمعارضة التي بعدها وهى التى سماها عند الجواب بالرابعة ، وهى أن المقدور لابد وأن يكون متميزا من غيره حتى يختص القادر بإيجاده فجوابها أن التميز العقلى كاف ، وجوابه بنفى الأمور النسبية غير نافع ههنا . والمعارضة الموسومة بالخامسة وهى أن تعاق القادر بالمقدور المطلق لايين ، وأما بالمقدور المعين فأمر اضافي وهو الذى سمى بالخالقية وحكمه حكم سائر الاضافات . والمعارضة الأخيرة بأن الموجدية صفة الموجد فهى إن كانت ممكنة الوجود وقمعت بالقادر عاد النقسم ، وار كانت واجبة وجوب وجود الأمر معه فجوابه ماقيل فى الصفات الإضافية.
And if we descend from asking for clarification, then why do you say that the well-ordered act indicates the knowledge of the doer, and its demonstration is from aspects. The second objection: that capability (al-makina) is not established in the state of occurrence because what is obtained at that time is necessary and its opposite is impossible in the present. This is refuted by what he mentioned: that what is obtained in the present is the ability to obtain in the future, except that this does not apply to the power of the servant with the statement that it is concomitant with the act. The investigation of it is that occurrence in the future is possible to co-exist with the existence of capability in the present, and impossible to co-exist with occurrence in the present, to the point that the impossible follows. The third objection: that the capable, according to you, is hesitant between doing and not doing, and not doing is not an object of power. Its answer is that it means that he can do and not do, not that he does not-do. The author mentioned in his answer what he mentioned in [the statement] 'The capable is the one for whom it is valid...' which is the answer to the second objection but in different wording. As for what he mentioned in the second type of objection, which is that the ability to influence (al-tamakkun min al-athar) requires the validity of the effect, its answer is that the one able to influence in eternity is contradictory, and therefore the one able to influence absolutely requires the validity of the effect after that. The objection that follows it, which he called in the answer the fourth, is that the object of power must be distinguished from others so that the capable is specific to bringing it into existence. Its answer is that mental distinction is sufficient, and answering by negating relational matters is not useful here. The objection labeled the fifth is that the relation of the capable to the absolute object of power is not [clear?], but to the specific object of power it is a relative matter, called the attribute of creating (al-khaliqiyya), and its ruling is the ruling of other relations. The last objection is that the property of existence (al-mujidiyya) is an attribute of the one who brings into existence. If it is possible in existence and subjugated to the capable, the division returns [؟], and if it is necessary, the obligation of the existence of the thing with it [؟]. Its answer is what was said about relational attributes.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers. After the initial proof, the author discusses why the well-ordered act indicates knowledge. He then lists objections systematically. The 'second objection' concerns capability (al-makina): an objector claims that if an act is already taking place, the capability to do it is not present because what is actual is necessary and its opposite impossible. The answer distinguishes between present capability for future acts; future occurrence is compatible with present capability. The 'third objection' says the capable is between doing and not-doing, but not-doing is not an object of power. The answer clarifies that 'able to do and not do' means being able to do or refrain, not that not-doing is a positive act. The author notes that this answer mirrors the second objection's answer in different words. Then a 'second type' (fourth?) about the ability to influence (tamakkun min al-athar) requiring the effect to be valid. The answer: ability in eternity is contradictory; absolute ability requires subsequent validity. The 'fourth objection' (as named) says the object of power must be distinct from the capable. Answer: mental distinction suffices; negating relational attributes does not help here. The 'fifth objection' concerns the relation of the capable to the absolute object of power vs. the specific object: the latter is a relative attribute (khaliqiyya). The 'last objection' asks about the property of existence (mujidiyya): is it possible or necessary? The answer appeals to the nature of relational attributes. The passage is dense and technical, showing the dialectical method of kalam.

التمكن
المقدور
القادر
الحصول
الاستقبال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Capability cannot exist at the same time as the act because the act is actual.
Correction: Capability is for future acts, so it coexists with future occurrence, not with present occurrence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, what is 'obtained in the present' (al-hasil fi al-hal)?
A) The act itself
B) The ability to obtain in the future
C) The impossibility of the opposite
D) The necessity of the act

Key Takeaway
Present capability is for future acts, not for acts already occurring.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that your present capability is for future acts. When you delay a good deed, do you truly believe you still have the capability to do it later, or do you assume that if it's not done now, the opportunity is lost forever? How does this lesson challenge your procrastination in seeking knowledge or performing acts of worship?
?
The objection implies that once an act occurs, its opposite is impossible. Have you ever felt trapped by your past mistakes, thinking you cannot change? But the refutation shows that present capability is for future acts. Are you using your past as an excuse to avoid future repentance? What specific step can you take tomorrow to break that cycle?
?
If your capability for good deeds is not about what you are doing right now but about what you can do later, are you actively planning to use that capability, or are you content with just having it? Write down one intention for an act of worship you will perform tomorrow, and commit to it with the awareness that your present capability is for that future act.

Objections on Hesitation and Influence, and Their Answers
Razi presents and refutes the third objection and the second type on influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection about the capable being hesitant between act and omission, and the objection about ability to influence requiring validity of effect, along with their answers.

Core Idea
The capable can do or refrain; ability to influence in eternity is contradictory.

After the second objection, the opponent continues with two more challenges. First, if the capable is between doing and not-doing, is not-doing an object of power? Second, if one has the ability to influence, does that imply the effect must be valid at that moment? Imam Razi provides nuanced answers that preserve the coherence of divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection and its answer
The objection: the capable is hesitant between doing and not-doing, but not-doing is not an object of power. The answer: it means he can do and not do, not that he does not-do.
This clarifies the meaning of hesitation.
2
Understand the author's note
The author notes that this answer mirrors the answer to the second objection in different wording.
3
Read the second type of objection
The objection: ability to influence (al-tamakkun min al-athar) requires the validity of the effect.
4
Grasp the answer about eternity
The one able to influence in eternity is contradictory; the absolute ability requires the validity of the effect after that.
This addresses the temporal aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولئن نزلنا عن الاستفسار فلم قلت إن فعل المحكم يدل على علم الفاعل وبيانه من وجوه .
والمعارضة الثانية بأن المنكنة لا تثبت في حال الحصول لأن الحاصل حينئذ واجب ومقابله ممتنع في الحال مدفوعة لما ذكره ، وهو أن الحاصل في الحال هو التمكن من التحصيل فى الاستقبال ، إلا أن ذلك لا يتمشى في قدرة العبد مع القول بكونها مقارنة للفعل .
والتحقيق فيه أن الوقوع في الاستقبال ممكن الإجتماع مع وجود المكنة في الحال وممتنع الاجتماع مع الوقوع في الحال حتى لزم منه المحال . والمعارضة الثالثة : بأن القادر على قولكم متردد بين الفعل والترك ، والترك لا يكون مقدوراً فجوابها أن منه أن يفعل وأن لا يفعل لا أن يفعل الترك ، والمصنف أورد في جوابه ما أورده في
القادر هو الذى يصح جواب المعارضة الثانية ولكن بعبارة أخرى .
وأما ما أورده في النوع الثاني من المعارضة وهو أن التمكن من الأثر يستدعى صحة الأثر ، فالجواب عنه
أن المتمكن من التأثير فى الأزل متناقض ولذلك كان المتمكن من التأثير مطلقا مستدعيا لصحة الأثر بعد ذلك
·
والمعارضة التي بعدها وهى التى سماها عند الجواب بالرابعة ، وهى أن المقدور لابد وأن يكون متميزا من غيره حتى يختص القادر بإيجاده فجوابها أن التميز العقلى كاف ، وجوابه بنفى الأمور النسبية غير نافع ههنا . والمعارضة الموسومة بالخامسة وهى أن تعاق القادر بالمقدور المطلق لايين ، وأما بالمقدور المعين فأمر اضافي وهو الذى سمى بالخالقية وحكمه حكم سائر الاضافات . والمعارضة الأخيرة بأن الموجدية صفة الموجد فهى إن كانت ممكنة الوجود وقمعت بالقادر عاد النقسم ، وار كانت واجبة وجوب وجود الأمر معه فجوابه ماقيل فى الصفات الإضافية.
And if we descend from asking for clarification, then why do you say that the well-ordered act indicates the knowledge of the doer, and its demonstration is from aspects. The second objection: that capability (al-makina) is not established in the state of occurrence because what is obtained at that time is necessary and its opposite is impossible in the present. This is refuted by what he mentioned: that what is obtained in the present is the ability to obtain in the future, except that this does not apply to the power of the servant with the statement that it is concomitant with the act. The investigation of it is that occurrence in the future is possible to co-exist with the existence of capability in the present, and impossible to co-exist with occurrence in the present, to the point that the impossible follows. The third objection: that the capable, according to you, is hesitant between doing and not doing, and not doing is not an object of power. Its answer is that it means that he can do and not do, not that he does not-do. The author mentioned in his answer what he mentioned in [the statement] 'The capable is the one for whom it is valid...' which is the answer to the second objection but in different wording. As for what he mentioned in the second type of objection, which is that the ability to influence (al-tamakkun min al-athar) requires the validity of the effect, its answer is that the one able to influence in eternity is contradictory, and therefore the one able to influence absolutely requires the validity of the effect after that. The objection that follows it, which he called in the answer the fourth, is that the object of power must be distinguished from others so that the capable is specific to bringing it into existence. Its answer is that mental distinction is sufficient, and answering by negating relational matters is not useful here. The objection labeled the fifth is that the relation of the capable to the absolute object of power is not [clear?], but to the specific object of power it is a relative matter, called the attribute of creating (al-khaliqiyya), and its ruling is the ruling of other relations. The last objection is that the property of existence (al-mujidiyya) is an attribute of the one who brings into existence. If it is possible in existence and subjugated to the capable, the division returns [؟], and if it is necessary, the obligation of the existence of the thing with it [؟]. Its answer is what was said about relational attributes.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers. After the initial proof, the author discusses why the well-ordered act indicates knowledge. He then lists objections systematically. The 'second objection' concerns capability (al-makina): an objector claims that if an act is already taking place, the capability to do it is not present because what is actual is necessary and its opposite impossible. The answer distinguishes between present capability for future acts; future occurrence is compatible with present capability. The 'third objection' says the capable is between doing and not-doing, but not-doing is not an object of power. The answer clarifies that 'able to do and not do' means being able to do or refrain, not that not-doing is a positive act. The author notes that this answer mirrors the second objection's answer in different words. Then a 'second type' (fourth?) about the ability to influence (tamakkun min al-athar) requiring the effect to be valid. The answer: ability in eternity is contradictory; absolute ability requires subsequent validity. The 'fourth objection' (as named) says the object of power must be distinct from the capable. Answer: mental distinction suffices; negating relational attributes does not help here. The 'fifth objection' concerns the relation of the capable to the absolute object of power vs. the specific object: the latter is a relative attribute (khaliqiyya). The 'last objection' asks about the property of existence (mujidiyya): is it possible or necessary? The answer appeals to the nature of relational attributes. The passage is dense and technical, showing the dialectical method of kalam.

Key Terms
القادر
الفعل والترك
المقدور
التمكن من الأثر
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Not-doing is a positive act that must be an object of power.
Correction: The capable can do or refrain; not-doing is not a separate object of power.

Checkpoint
How does the answer to the third objection clarify the meaning of 'able to do and not do'?
A) It means he can do both simultaneously.
B) It means he can do or refrain, not that not-doing is a positive act.
C) It means not-doing is a separate object of power.
D) It means he is not really hesitant.

Key Takeaway
The capable's hesitation means the ability to do or refrain, not that refraining is a positive act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah's ability to do or refrain is not a limitation, does your heart truly rest in His absolute power? Reflect on a recent dua where you felt He must answer. Commit to repenting of any subtle expectation that He must act in a certain way.
?
The lesson shows that inability to influence in eternity is a logical necessity, not a weakness. Do you ever doubt Allah's power when He delays answering your prayers? Ask yourself why you expect immediate effect. Resolve to trust His timing completely.
?
If refraining is not a positive act, then Allah's not creating something is not a deficiency. Are you ever disappointed when you don't receive what you desire? Is that disappointment rooted in a lack of contentment with His choice? Make a firm intention to accept His wisdom even when it contradicts your wishes.

Objections on Distinction, Relation, and Existence, and Their Answers
Razi presents and refutes the fourth, fifth, and last objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth, fifth, and final objections regarding distinction of the object of power, the relation of the capable to specific objects, and the nature of the attribute of existence, along with their answers.

Core Idea
Mental distinction suffices for power; relational attributes like khaliqiyya are relative; mujidiyya is a relational attribute.

The opponent raises three more objections designed to undermine the structure of divine power. They concern the need for the object of power to be distinct, the nature of the relation between the capable and specific acts, and the status of the attribute of bringing into existence. Imam Razi’s answers rely on the concept of mental distinction and the nature of relational attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth objection and answer
The objection: the object of power must be distinguished from others. The answer: mental distinction is sufficient.
2
Understand why mental distinction suffices
The answer notes that negating relational matters is not useful here; mental distinction is enough.
3
Read the fifth objection on khaliqiyya
The objection: the relation to the absolute object is unclear; to the specific object it is a relative matter called khaliqiyya.
4
Read the last objection on mujidiyya
The objection: the attribute of bringing into existence (mujidiyya) is either possible or necessary. The answer refers to relational attributes.
5
Grasp the general answer about relational attributes
All these objections are answered by appealing to the nature of relational attributes (sifat idafiyya).

Misconception & Correction

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's attribute of being 'Creator' is relational—meaning it ties Him directly to your existence at every moment—does that change how you view your dependence on Him? Will you consciously acknowledge His ongoing act of bringing you into existence today?
?
When you face a situation that seems beyond God's control, are you tempted to doubt His power over that specific event? How does knowing that even His 'bringing into existence' is a relational attribute reassure you that He is intimately involved? What specific concern will you entrust to Him right now?

Objections from Ignorant Agents and Animal Instincts
Imam Razi presents and refutes two objections to the proof from cosmic order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why examples of ignorant people and animals performing ordered acts do not disprove the argument for God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Ordered acts always stem from appropriate knowledge, even if instinctive.

The proof for God's knowledge based on cosmic order faces counterexamples: an ignorant person may occasionally produce a well-ordered act, and animals like bees and spiders display intricate behavior without apparent knowledge. Imam Razi addresses these objections by showing that such examples actually confirm the presence of knowledge, albeit limited. This lesson examines the objections and their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from the ignorant agent
Examine the claim that an ignorant person can occasionally produce a well-ordered act, and if one instance is possible, then multiple are possible.
2
Read the objection from animal instincts
Consider bees and spiders as examples of complex ordered behavior without apparent knowledge.
3
Study the answers
Imam Razi distinguishes between occasional acts and consistent order, and asserts that animals have instinctive knowledge of their specific acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أن الجاهل قد يتفق منه الفعل المحكم نادراً واتفق المقلا على أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله فلما جاز ذلك مرة واحدة جاز أيضا مرتين وثلاثا وأربعا .
وثانيها أن قبل النحلة في غاية الاحكام وهو بناء البيوت المسدسة مع كثرة ما فيها من الحكمة التي لا يعرفها إلا المهندسون ، وكذا العنكبوت تبنى بيتها في غاية الاحكام ، وكذلك نرى كل واحد من الحيوانات تأتى بالأفعال المرافقة لها بحيث يعجز عن تحصيلها أكثر الأذكياء مع أنه ليس بشيء منها علم ولا حكمة ، ولئن سلمنا أن ما ذكرته يدل على كونه تعالى عالما اسكنه معارض بأمرين الأول أن كونه عالما بالشيء نسبة بينه وبين ذلك الشيء فتلك النسبة غير ذاته لا محالة والموصوف بها والمقتضى لها هو ذاته تعالى فيكون الشيء الواحد قابلا وفاعلا وهى محال ، أما أولا فلان البسيط لا يصدر عنه إلا أثر واحد ، وأما ثانيا فان نسبة القبول بالامكان ونسبة التأثير بالوجوب والنسبة الواحدة لا تكون بالامكان والوجوب ..ا .
الثانى : أن العلم إن لم يكن صفة كمال وجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه ، وإن كان صفة كمال كان الله تعالى محتاجا فى استفادة الكمال إلى تلك السفة والكامل بغيره ناقص بذاته والمحتاج إلى الغير ناقص اذاته أيضا وذلك على الله تعالى محال ، والجواب أما الكلام في الواسطة فقد تقدم وأما الإحكام فالمراد منه الترتيب العجيب والتأليف اللطيف ولا يشك أن العالم كذلك .
قوله : إذا جاز صدور الفعل المحكم عن الجاهل مرة واحدة فليجز مرارا كثيرة .
قلنا بديهة العقل بعد الاستقراء شاهدة بالفرق، وأما الحيوانات فكل من فعل فعلا محكما فهو عالم بذلك الفعل فقط
وأما المعارضة الأولى جوابها لم لا يجوز كون الشيء الواحد قابلا ومؤثراً .
له : الواحد لا يكون مصدراً لأثرين .
قلنا تقدم إبطاله .
قوله النسبة الواحدة لا تكون بالإمكان والوجوب معا .
قلنا نسبة القبول بالإمكان العام وهو لا ينا فى نسبة الوجوب ، وأما حديث الكمال والنقصان فخطابي وهو
معارض بما تقرر في البداية أن صفة العلم صفة كمال والجهل صفة تقصان وتعالى الله عن النقصان
One of them: that the ignorant may occasionally produce a well-ordered act, and the rational people agreed that the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like, so when it is permissible once, it is also permissible twice, thrice, and four times. The second: that the bee builds its hive with utmost perfection, building hexagonal houses with much wisdom that only engineers know, and likewise the spider builds its web with utmost perfection, and likewise we see each animal performing acts suited to it such that most intelligent people are incapable of achieving them, and none of them has knowledge or wisdom. And if we concede that what you mentioned indicates that He is knowing, then it is opposed by two matters: The first is that His being knowing of a thing is a relation between Him and that thing, and that relation is other than His essence necessarily, and the one described by it and the one that necessitates it is His essence, so the single thing becomes both receiver and agent, which is impossible. Firstly, because the simple does not emanate but one effect. Secondly, because the relation of receiving is by possibility (imkan) and the relation of effecting is by necessity (wujub), and a single relation cannot be by possibility and necessity together. The second: that if knowledge is not an attribute of perfection, then it is necessary to exonerate God from it, and if it is an attribute of perfection, then God is in need of that attribute to acquire perfection, and the perfect by other than itself is deficient in itself, and the one in need of another is also deficient in itself, and that is impossible for God. The answer: as for the discussion about the intermediary, it has preceded, and as for well-ordered (ihkam), what is meant is the wonderful arrangement and subtle composition, and there is no doubt that the world is like that. He said: if it is permissible for a well-ordered act to emanate from the ignorant once, then it is permissible many times. We say: the clear reason after induction testifies to the difference. As for animals, whoever performs a well-ordered act is knowledgeable of that act only. As for the first objection, its answer is: why is it not permissible for a single thing to be both receiver and agent? He says: the one cannot be source of two effects. We say: its refutation has preceded. He said: a single relation cannot be both by possibility and necessity together. We say: the relation of receiving is by absolute possibility (al-imkan al-amm), and that does not contradict the relation of necessity. As for the discussion of perfection and deficiency, it is rhetorical, and it is opposed by what is established in the beginning: that the attribute of knowledge is an attribute of perfection and ignorance is an attribute of deficiency, and God is exalted above deficiency.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents two counterexamples and two philosophical objections, with answers. The first counterexample: an ignorant person may occasionally produce a well-ordered act, and if one instance is possible, then multiple instances are possible, undermining the proof. The second: bees and spiders exhibit highly ordered behavior without apparent knowledge. The author then presents two philosophical objections to divine knowledge: 1) If God's knowledge is a relation, it implies multiplicity in God, and the simple cannot produce multiple effects or have relational multiplicity; also, the relation of receiving (by possibility) and effecting (by necessity) cannot coexist in one relation. 2) If knowledge is a perfection, then God would need it, implying deficiency; if it is not a perfection, then God must be above it. The answers: For the first counterexample, reason after induction shows a difference (the ignorant person cannot consistently produce ordered acts). For animals, each animal is 'knowledgeable' in its specific act (instinctive knowledge). The first philosophical objection is answered by reinterpreting: a single thing can be both receiver and agent; the claim that the one cannot cause two effects is refuted earlier. The objection about possibility and necessity is answered by distinguishing absolute possibility (al-imkan al-amm) which is compatible with necessity in the context. The perfection argument is dismissed as rhetorical and countered by the established principle that knowledge is perfection and ignorance deficiency; hence God's knowledge is not a need but a perfection proper to Him.

Key Terms
الجاهل
المُحكَم
النحلة
العنكبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi respond to the objection that an ignorant person can produce a well-ordered act?
A) He denies that the act is truly well-ordered.
B) He says that reason after induction shows a difference between occasional and consistent order.
C) He concedes the point but argues that it doesn't affect the proof.
D) He asserts that the ignorant person must have hidden knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Examples of ignorant agents and animals do not disprove divine knowledge; they show that order always stems from some form of knowledge, whether instinctive or rational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the perfect symmetry of a spider's web or the precise geometry of a honeycomb, do you ever reduce it to mere 'instinct' and forget that instinct itself is a gift of knowledge from Allah? How would your heart change if you habitually recognized His knowledge behind every ordered pattern?
?
Have you ever used rare exceptions—like a lucky guess or a fluke—to rationalize away the overwhelming evidence of design in the universe? What would it cost you to admit that your excuses are weaker than the consistent order Allah has placed before you?
?
If an animal's instinctive act is actual knowledge from its Creator, then what does your own intentional ignorance or neglect of divine signs say about your responsibility? Will you today commit to seeing every intricate detail as a direct lesson in His omniscience?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Philosophical Argument on Relation and Multiplicity in God
Imam Razi presents the first philosophical argument against divine knowledge and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's knowledge as a relation implies multiplicity in the simple essence, and Imam Razi's reply that a single thing can be both receiver and agent.

Core Idea
A simple essence can be both receiver and agent without multiplicity.

A philosophical objection argues that if God's knowledge is a relation between Him and objects, then that relation is distinct from His essence, making His essence both the subject and cause of the relation — implying multiplicity in the simple God. Additionally, the relation of receiving (by possibility) and effecting (by necessity) cannot coexist. Imam Razi answers by affirming that a single thing can be both receiver and agent, and by distinguishing absolute possibility from necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that knowledge as a relation creates a distinction in God, violating His simplicity, and that the simple cannot produce multiple effects.
2
Examine the second part of the objection
The relation of receiving is by possibility, and effecting by necessity; a single relation cannot be both.
3
Study Imam Razi's answer
He answers that a single thing can be both receiver and agent, and that absolute possibility (al-imkan al-amm) does not contradict necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أن الجاهل قد يتفق منه الفعل المحكم نادراً واتفق المقلا على أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله فلما جاز ذلك مرة واحدة جاز أيضا مرتين وثلاثا وأربعا .
وثانيها أن قبل النحلة في غاية الاحكام وهو بناء البيوت المسدسة مع كثرة ما فيها من الحكمة التي لا يعرفها إلا المهندسون ، وكذا العنكبوت تبنى بيتها في غاية الاحكام ، وكذلك نرى كل واحد من الحيوانات تأتى بالأفعال المرافقة لها بحيث يعجز عن تحصيلها أكثر الأذكياء مع أنه ليس بشيء منها علم ولا حكمة ، ولئن سلمنا أن ما ذكرته يدل على كونه تعالى عالما اسكنه معارض بأمرين الأول أن كونه عالما بالشيء نسبة بينه وبين ذلك الشيء فتلك النسبة غير ذاته لا محالة والموصوف بها والمقتضى لها هو ذاته تعالى فيكون الشيء الواحد قابلا وفاعلا وهى محال ، أما أولا فلان البسيط لا يصدر عنه إلا أثر واحد ، وأما ثانيا فان نسبة القبول بالامكان ونسبة التأثير بالوجوب والنسبة الواحدة لا تكون بالامكان والوجوب ..ا .
الثانى : أن العلم إن لم يكن صفة كمال وجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه ، وإن كان صفة كمال كان الله تعالى محتاجا فى استفادة الكمال إلى تلك السفة والكامل بغيره ناقص بذاته والمحتاج إلى الغير ناقص اذاته أيضا وذلك على الله تعالى محال ، والجواب أما الكلام في الواسطة فقد تقدم وأما الإحكام فالمراد منه الترتيب العجيب والتأليف اللطيف ولا يشك أن العالم كذلك .
قوله : إذا جاز صدور الفعل المحكم عن الجاهل مرة واحدة فليجز مرارا كثيرة .
قلنا بديهة العقل بعد الاستقراء شاهدة بالفرق، وأما الحيوانات فكل من فعل فعلا محكما فهو عالم بذلك الفعل فقط
وأما المعارضة الأولى جوابها لم لا يجوز كون الشيء الواحد قابلا ومؤثراً .
له : الواحد لا يكون مصدراً لأثرين .
قلنا تقدم إبطاله .
قوله النسبة الواحدة لا تكون بالإمكان والوجوب معا .
قلنا نسبة القبول بالإمكان العام وهو لا ينا فى نسبة الوجوب ، وأما حديث الكمال والنقصان فخطابي وهو
معارض بما تقرر في البداية أن صفة العلم صفة كمال والجهل صفة تقصان وتعالى الله عن النقصان
One of them: that the ignorant may occasionally produce a well-ordered act, and the rational people agreed that the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like, so when it is permissible once, it is also permissible twice, thrice, and four times. The second: that the bee builds its hive with utmost perfection, building hexagonal houses with much wisdom that only engineers know, and likewise the spider builds its web with utmost perfection, and likewise we see each animal performing acts suited to it such that most intelligent people are incapable of achieving them, and none of them has knowledge or wisdom. And if we concede that what you mentioned indicates that He is knowing, then it is opposed by two matters: The first is that His being knowing of a thing is a relation between Him and that thing, and that relation is other than His essence necessarily, and the one described by it and the one that necessitates it is His essence, so the single thing becomes both receiver and agent, which is impossible. Firstly, because the simple does not emanate but one effect. Secondly, because the relation of receiving is by possibility (imkan) and the relation of effecting is by necessity (wujub), and a single relation cannot be by possibility and necessity together. The second: that if knowledge is not an attribute of perfection, then it is necessary to exonerate God from it, and if it is an attribute of perfection, then God is in need of that attribute to acquire perfection, and the perfect by other than itself is deficient in itself, and the one in need of another is also deficient in itself, and that is impossible for God. The answer: as for the discussion about the intermediary, it has preceded, and as for well-ordered (ihkam), what is meant is the wonderful arrangement and subtle composition, and there is no doubt that the world is like that. He said: if it is permissible for a well-ordered act to emanate from the ignorant once, then it is permissible many times. We say: the clear reason after induction testifies to the difference. As for animals, whoever performs a well-ordered act is knowledgeable of that act only. As for the first objection, its answer is: why is it not permissible for a single thing to be both receiver and agent? He says: the one cannot be source of two effects. We say: its refutation has preceded. He said: a single relation cannot be both by possibility and necessity together. We say: the relation of receiving is by absolute possibility (al-imkan al-amm), and that does not contradict the relation of necessity. As for the discussion of perfection and deficiency, it is rhetorical, and it is opposed by what is established in the beginning: that the attribute of knowledge is an attribute of perfection and ignorance is an attribute of deficiency, and God is exalted above deficiency.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents two counterexamples and two philosophical objections, with answers. The first counterexample: an ignorant person may occasionally produce a well-ordered act, and if one instance is possible, then multiple instances are possible, undermining the proof. The second: bees and spiders exhibit highly ordered behavior without apparent knowledge. The author then presents two philosophical objections to divine knowledge: 1) If God's knowledge is a relation, it implies multiplicity in God, and the simple cannot produce multiple effects or have relational multiplicity; also, the relation of receiving (by possibility) and effecting (by necessity) cannot coexist in one relation. 2) If knowledge is a perfection, then God would need it, implying deficiency; if it is not a perfection, then God must be above it. The answers: For the first counterexample, reason after induction shows a difference (the ignorant person cannot consistently produce ordered acts). For animals, each animal is 'knowledgeable' in its specific act (instinctive knowledge). The first philosophical objection is answered by reinterpreting: a single thing can be both receiver and agent; the claim that the one cannot cause two effects is refuted earlier. The objection about possibility and necessity is answered by distinguishing absolute possibility (al-imkan al-amm) which is compatible with necessity in the context. The perfection argument is dismissed as rhetorical and countered by the established principle that knowledge is perfection and ignorance deficiency; hence God's knowledge is not a need but a perfection proper to Him.

القابل
المؤثر
الإمكان العام
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge as a relation necessarily creates multiplicity in God.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that a single essence can be both receiver and agent without contradiction, and absolute possibility is compatible with necessity.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi say about the objection that a single relation cannot be both by possibility and necessity?
A) He agrees and concedes the point.
B) He says that the relation of receiving is by absolute possibility, which does not contradict necessity.
C) He argues that the relation is not real but mental.
D) He claims that necessity overrides possibility.

Key Takeaway
The objection that knowledge implies multiplicity is answered by affirming that a single essence can be both receiver and agent, and by distinguishing absolute possibility from necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's single essence encompasses all attributes without division or multiplicity, does that change how you perceive His knowledge of your every thought and action? Will you trust His comprehensive knowledge more deeply today?
?
The objection claims a simple essence cannot have multiple relations. Reflect on a time you struggled to reconcile two attributes of God (e.g., mercy and justice). How does Razi’s argument about absolute possibility help you trust that God’s essence is not conflicted? Will you actively work to remove any intellectual doubt about His oneness from your heart?
?
The relation of receiving is based on absolute possibility. How does it affect your worship to know that God is both the One who knows you (receiver of the relation) and the One who created you (agent)? Does this drive you to increase your awareness that He is always present? What concrete step will you take today to live with that awareness?

Philosophical Argument on Knowledge as Perfection or Deficiency
Imam Razi presents and refutes the second philosophical argument against divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if knowledge is a perfection, then God would be dependent on it, implying deficiency, and Imam Razi's reply that knowledge is inherently a perfection and God's possession of it does not denote need.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not a need but a perfection befitting His essence.

The second philosophical argument presents a dilemma: if knowledge is an attribute of perfection, then God would need it to acquire perfection, making Him dependent; if it is not a perfection, then God must be exonerated from it, implying ignorance, which is also impossible. Imam Razi dismisses this as rhetorical and counters by affirming that knowledge is inherently a perfection and ignorance a deficiency, and God is transcendent above deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the dilemma
Understand the two horns: either knowledge is a perfection (then God needs it) or not (then God lacks it). Both seem problematic.
2
Read Imam Razi's answer
He calls the argument rhetorical and points to the established principle that knowledge is perfection, ignorance deficiency.
3
Reflect on the broader context
This shows that the attribute of knowledge is rooted in the divine essence without implying need.

Source Text

Why Ancient Philosophers Denied Knowledge to God
Razi explains the philosophers' reasoning for denying knowledge to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ancient philosophers denied that God has knowledge, based on their definition of knowledge and the absolute simplicity of the First Principle.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is impossible for philosophers because it introduces multiplicity into the simple First Principle.

Imam Razi begins by presenting the argument of the ancient Greek philosophers that God cannot be described with knowledge. Their reasoning is based on a specific definition of knowledge as the mental form of the known, which would require a relation and thus multiplicity in God—contradicting His absolute simplicity. Let's follow their logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the structure: definition, two cases, conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the definition of knowledge
The philosophers define knowledge as the acquisition of the form of the known in the knower (صورة المعلوم في العالم). This definition implies a relation between knower and known.
3
Follow the two cases
If the knower and known are distinct (case 1), the knower must have the form of the known—impossible for the First Principle. If they are one (case 2), there must be a distinction of considerations, implying multiplicity—also impossible.
Both cases lead to the same problem.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the First Principle is absolutely simple with no multiplicity, He cannot have knowledge. Instead, He emanates knowledge to His effects.
This is the core of their doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: قدماء الفلاسفة قالوا العلم حصول صورة المعلوم في العالم ومع ذلك فهو يقتضى إضافة ما للعالم إلى المعلوم ، والعالم والمعلوم إن كانا متغايرين فلابد أن يتصور العالم بصورة المعلوم ولا يمكن أن يعقل المبدأ الأول شيئا من غيره ، وإن كان واحداً فلابد فيه من تغاير اعتبارين حتى يمكن أن يعقل الإضافة بينهما ولا كثرة في المبدأ الأول بوجه من الوجوه فهو لا يوصف بالعلم بوجه بل هو نقيض العلم على الموجودات التي هي معلولاته بل هو يفيض العلم على الموجودات التي هي معلولاته كما يفيض الوجود عليها بهذا مذهبهم . والباقون منا ومن أهل الملل جميعا انفقوا على أنه تعالى عالم ، أما الإحكام والإتقان فقد يظهر لمن يتأمل أحوال الخلق وينظر في تشريح الأعضاء ومنافعها وهيئة الإفلاك ووجود الذيرات العلوية وحركاتها ، وبديها
I say: The ancient philosophers said that knowledge is the acquisition of the form of the known in the knower, and with that it necessitates some relation of the knower to the known. If the knower and the known are distinct, then the knower must be imprinted with the form of the known, and it is impossible for the First Principle to conceive anything other than itself. And if they are one, then there must be a distinction of two considerations so that the relation between them can be conceived, and there is no multiplicity in the First Principle in any respect, so He is not described by knowledge at all; rather, He is the opposite of knowledge regarding the existents that are His effects; rather, He emanates knowledge upon the existents that are His effects just as He emanates existence upon them. This is their doctrine. The rest of us and all the people of religions agree that He is knowing. As for well-ordered and precise, it appears to whoever contemplates the states of creation and looks at the anatomy of limbs and their benefits, the configuration of the spheres, the existence of celestial particles and their movements, and the evident (or: and [it is] self-evident) ...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a footnote or commentary by Imam Razi explaining the position of ancient Greek philosophers on divine knowledge. They define knowledge as the mental form of the known, which requires a relation. If God knows other things, He must have forms of them, implying multiplicity. If He knows only Himself, then there must be a distinction of considerations, again implying multiplicity. Since the First Principle is absolutely simple, He cannot have knowledge of anything other than Himself. Therefore, God is not described with knowledge; rather, He emanates knowledge to His effects. The author then states that the rest of us (Muslims and all religious people) agree that God is knowing. He asserts that the evidence of well-ordered and precise design in the world is apparent to anyone who contemplates creation, anatomy, the heavens, etc. This underscores that the consensus of religious people, based on empirical evidence, overrides the philosophical objection.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة
العلم
صورة المعلوم
المبدأ الأول
التغاير الاعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ancient philosophers denied God's knowledge because they thought God is ignorant or imperfect.
Correction: Their denial is based on a specific metaphysical definition of knowledge and God's absolute simplicity, not on a belief that God lacks perfection.

Checkpoint
Why do the ancient philosophers deny that God is described with knowledge?
A) Because knowledge requires a relation between knower and known, which would introduce multiplicity in God.
B) Because they believe God is ignorant.
C) Because knowledge is imperfect and God is perfect.
D) Because they deny the existence of God.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' denial of divine knowledge stems from their definition of knowledge as mental impression and God's absolute simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you unconsciously imagine it as a 'form' or 'image' in His mind, like human knowledge? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what does it reveal about the limitations of your own conception? Will you actively work to purify your understanding of God's attributes from any trace of human analogy?
?
The philosophers argued that God's simplicity means He cannot know His creation without multiplicity. Have you ever felt that God's omniscience makes Him distant or impersonal, as if He cannot truly engage with you without being 'affected'? How does the Islamic belief that God knows all without composition change the way you approach Him in prayer? Are you willing to surrender your own need for a 'relatable' God and accept His transcendence?
?
If God's knowledge were like ours, He would be dependent on His creation to have something to know. Do you live as if God needs your worship or your existence to be complete? What would it mean for your daily life to truly believe that God is absolutely independent and His knowledge is perfect without any external input? Will you let this truth transform your humility and reliance on Him alone?

The Religious Consensus on Divine Knowledge and the Evidence of Design
Razi states the religious consensus and cites design as evidence for God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all religious people agree God is knowing and that the observable design in creation provides clear evidence of His knowledge.

Core Idea
The intricate design of creation is clear evidence that God is all-knowing.

After presenting the philosophers' objection, Imam Razi states that all religious people—Muslims and others—unanimously affirm that God is knowing. He then points to the visible perfection and precision in the universe, from human anatomy to the celestial spheres, as proof that anyone can see. This empirical evidence directly counters the abstract philosophical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the emphatic phrase 'all of us and all people of religions'.
This is a strong claim of universal agreement among believers.
2
Identify the evidence cited
Razi mentions contemplation of creation, anatomy of limbs, benefits of organs, configuration of spheres, and celestial particles with their movements.
These are examples of design (الإحكام والإتقان).
3
Connect evidence to the refutation
Reflect on how this observable perfection refutes the philosophers' claim that God cannot have knowledge. The design implies a knowing designer.
This is a standard argument from design.
4
Appreciate the contrast
The philosophers' argument is abstract and theoretical, while the religious evidence is empirical and accessible to anyone who reflects.

أقول: قدماء الفلاسفة قالوا العلم حصول صورة المعلوم في العالم ومع ذلك فهو يقتضى إضافة ما للعالم إلى المعلوم ، والعالم والمعلوم إن كانا متغايرين فلابد أن يتصور العالم بصورة المعلوم ولا يمكن أن يعقل المبدأ الأول شيئا من غيره ، وإن كان واحداً فلابد فيه من تغاير اعتبارين حتى يمكن أن يعقل الإضافة بينهما ولا كثرة في المبدأ الأول بوجه من الوجوه فهو لا يوصف بالعلم بوجه بل هو نقيض العلم على الموجودات التي هي معلولاته بل هو يفيض العلم على الموجودات التي هي معلولاته كما يفيض الوجود عليها بهذا مذهبهم . والباقون منا ومن أهل الملل جميعا انفقوا على أنه تعالى عالم ، أما الإحكام والإتقان فقد يظهر لمن يتأمل أحوال الخلق وينظر في تشريح الأعضاء ومنافعها وهيئة الإفلاك ووجود الذيرات العلوية وحركاتها ، وبديها
I say: The ancient philosophers said that knowledge is the acquisition of the form of the known in the knower, and with that it necessitates some relation of the knower to the known. If the knower and the known are distinct, then the knower must be imprinted with the form of the known, and it is impossible for the First Principle to conceive anything other than itself. And if they are one, then there must be a distinction of two considerations so that the relation between them can be conceived, and there is no multiplicity in the First Principle in any respect, so He is not described by knowledge at all; rather, He is the opposite of knowledge regarding the existents that are His effects; rather, He emanates knowledge upon the existents that are His effects just as He emanates existence upon them. This is their doctrine. The rest of us and all the people of religions agree that He is knowing. As for well-ordered and precise, it appears to whoever contemplates the states of creation and looks at the anatomy of limbs and their benefits, the configuration of the spheres, the existence of celestial particles and their movements, and the evident (or: and [it is] self-evident) ...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a footnote or commentary by Imam Razi explaining the position of ancient Greek philosophers on divine knowledge. They define knowledge as the mental form of the known, which requires a relation. If God knows other things, He must have forms of them, implying multiplicity. If He knows only Himself, then there must be a distinction of considerations, again implying multiplicity. Since the First Principle is absolutely simple, He cannot have knowledge of anything other than Himself. Therefore, God is not described with knowledge; rather, He emanates knowledge to His effects. The author then states that the rest of us (Muslims and all religious people) agree that God is knowing. He asserts that the evidence of well-ordered and precise design in the world is apparent to anyone who contemplates creation, anatomy, the heavens, etc. This underscores that the consensus of religious people, based on empirical evidence, overrides the philosophical objection.

Key Terms
الإحكام والإتقان
الخلق
تشريح الأعضاء
الإفلاك
الذيرات العلوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What evidence does Imam Razi cite for God's knowledge?
A) The beauty of nature and the complexity of ecosystems.
B) The anatomy of limbs, the configuration of spheres, and celestial particles.
C) The miracles of prophets and the Quran.
D) The consensus of philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The universal consensus of religious people and the observable design in creation affirm that God is all-knowing.

?
The source says the design is evident to 'whoever contemplates the states of creation.' When did you last pause to contemplate the intricate design of even one part of nature? What would change in your daily life if you made that contemplation a regular habit?
?
Razi mentions that all religious people agree on God's knowledge. Does your belief in God's knowledge actually affect how you live—your decisions, your trust in Him, your fear of His awareness—or is it just a theoretical statement you accept without transformation?

Objection: Knowledge as a Relation — Subject and Agent Unity
Razi presents an objection and two-part response about knowledge as a relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if knowledge is a relation, God's essence would be both recipient and agent, and Razi's response using the concept of general possibility.

Core Idea
A simple essence can be both recipient and agent through different aspects of possibility.

In this passage, an objector challenges the previous proof that God is knowing by arguing that knowledge as a relation leads to a contradiction: the same simple essence would be both the recipient and the agent of that relation. Razi refutes this by invoking the distinction between general possibility and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's claim that knowledge is a relation between God and the known, making God's essence both the subject qualified by the relation and the cause of that relation.
This is the core of the objection: the same thing acting as both recipient and agent.
2
Identify the two reasons
Note the two supporting arguments: (1) a simple essence can only produce one effect, and (2) reception involves possibility while causation involves necessity, and a single relation cannot have both modalities.
These reasons aim to show the impossibility of the same essence being both.
3
Follow Razi's response
Razi counters each reason: first, why cannot one thing be both recipient and agent? The objector reasserts the unity issue, but Razi says it has been refuted. Second, Razi clarifies that reception involves 'general possibility' which does not conflict with necessity.
General possibility is a broader concept that allows compatibility.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Realize how this defense preserves God's simplicity while allowing multiple relations. The key is distinguishing different aspects of possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولئن سلمنا أن ما ذكرته يدل على كونه تعالى عالما اسكنه معارض بأمرين الأول أن كونه عالما بالشيء نسبة بينه وبين ذلك الشيء فتلك النسبة غير ذاته لا محالة والموصوف بها والمقتضى لها هو ذاته تعالى فيكون الشيء الواحد قابلا وفاعلا وهى محال ، أما أولا فلان البسيط لا يصدر عنه إلا أثر واحد ، وأما ثانيا فان نسبة القبول بالامكان ونسبة التأثير بالوجوب والنسبة الواحدة لا تكون بالامكان والوجوب ..ا .
وأما المعارضة الأولى جوابها لم لا يجوز كون الشيء الواحد قابلا ومؤثراً . له : الواحد لا يكون مصدراً لأثرين . قلنا تقدم إبطاله . قوله النسبة الواحدة لا تكون بالإمكان والوجوب معا . قلنا نسبة القبول بالإمكان العام وهو لا ينا فى نسبة الوجوب .
And if we grant that what you mentioned indicates that He is knowing, it is opposed by two matters: first, that His being knowing of a thing is a relation between Him and that thing; that relation is other than His essence necessarily, and the described by it and the necessitator of it is His essence—so the one thing would be both recipient and agent, which is impossible. First, because the simple cannot originate except one effect. Second, because the relation of reception is with possibility and the relation of effecting is with necessity, and a single relation cannot be with possibility and necessity... As for the first objection, its response: why is it not permissible for one thing to be both recipient and agent? To him: the one cannot be the source of two effects. We said its refutation preceded. His statement: a single relation cannot be both with possibility and necessity. We say: the relation of reception is with general possibility, and it does not oppose the relation of necessity.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two sub-objections (under 'ولئن سلمنا') and its answer. The objector argues: if knowledge is a relation between knower and known, that relation is other than God's essence. God's essence is both the subject qualified by it and the cause of it—thus the same thing would be both recipient (قابل) and agent (فاعل), which is impossible. Two reasons: (1) a simple essence produces only one effect; (2) reception requires possibility (الإمكان) while causation requires necessity (الوجوب), and a single relation cannot have both. Razi responds: first, why cannot one thing be both recipient and agent? The objector then reasserts the unity issue: the one cannot be source of two effects. Razi says this has already been refuted. As for the second point, Razi says the relation of reception is with general possibility (الإمكان العام), and that does not conflict with the relation of necessity.

Key Terms
نسبة
القبول
التأثير
الإمكان العام
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A simple essence cannot have multiple relations because it can only produce one effect.
Correction: Razi argues that general possibility allows a simple essence to be both recipient (through possibility) and agent (through necessity) without contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's response to the claim that a single relation cannot be both possible and necessary?
A) He denies the distinction between possibility and necessity.
B) He says the relation of reception is with general possibility, which does not oppose necessity.
C) He agrees but says God's essence is not simple.
D) He uses a different argument about knowledge being a perfection.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends God's simplicity by arguing that the relation of reception involves general possibility, which does not conflict with necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objector claimed that a simple being cannot be both recipient and agent. Where in your own life do you try to compartmentalize God—seeing Him as either passive or active, but not both? Will you today worship Him as the One whose simplicity embraces all relations without contradiction?
?
If general possibility allows God to have relations without compromising His unity, then what does that mean for how you relate to Him? Can you accept that your prayers and actions affect no change in Him, yet He is truly the One who hears and responds? How will you live differently knowing that your reception of His mercy and His causation of it are perfectly compatible in His simple essence?

Objection: Knowledge as Perfection Implies Dependence
Razi presents an objection and then refutes it with a key distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends God's knowledge as a perfection without implying that God depends on another for perfection.

Core Idea
God's perfection is inherent, not acquired; His attributes are perfect because they are His.

This passage presents an objection against attributing knowledge to God, arguing that if knowledge is a perfection, then God would be in need of it. Razi first dismisses this as rhetorical but then provides a deeper answer distinguishing between deficient and perfect essences. Let's follow the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part: if knowledge is not a perfection, God must be exalted above it; but if it is a perfection, God would need it to acquire perfection, implying He is perfect through another and thus deficient in Himself.
This is the standard dilemma against perfection attributes.
2
Examine the initial rebuttal
Razi calls this talk of perfection and deficiency 'rhetorical' and cites an established principle that knowledge is perfection and ignorance deficiency.
This rebuttal is brief and not fully satisfying.
3
Discover the stronger objection
The objector counters that even if knowledge is a perfection, God cannot derive perfection from an attribute, as that still implies dependence on another.
The objector agrees that knowledge is perfection but insists the dependence problem remains.
4
Grasp the final answer
Razi distinguishes between deficient essences (ذوات ناقصة) which need attributes to perfect themselves, and perfect essences (ذوات كاملة) like God, whose attributes are perfect because they are attributes of that perfect essence. Knowledge's perfection is due to its being God's attribute.
This answer resolves the objection by showing that for God, attributes do not confer separate perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانى : أن العلم إن لم يكن صفة كمال وجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه ، وإن كان صفة كمال كان الله تعالى محتاجا فى استفادة الكمال إلى تلك السفة والكامل بغيره ناقص بذاته والمحتاج إلى الغير ناقص اذاته أيضا وذلك على الله تعالى محال .
وأما حديث الكمال والنقصان فخطابي وهو معارض بما تقرر في البداية أن صفة العلم صفة كمال والجهل صفة تقصان وتعالى الله عن النقصان .
والمعارضة الثانية : بأن العلم كمال ولا يمكن أن الله تعالى يستفيد الكمال من غيره فليس جوابه أنه خطابي ولا يندفع بقوله إن العلم كمال والجهل نقصان وتعالى ذاته عن النقصان ، فإن القائل يقول وتعالى الله عن الاستفادة كمالا عن غيره أيضا ونفى الاستفادة عن الله ليس بخطابي . والجواب أن الذوات الناقصة تستفيد للكمال من صفاتها الكاملة ، أما الذوات الكاملة وصفاته الإنما تكون كاملة لكونها صفات لتلك الذوات وكمال العلم من هذا النوع ، فإن سبب كماله كونه من صفات الله تعالى .
Second: that if knowledge is not a perfection, then God must be exalted above it; but if it is a perfection, then God would be in need of acquiring perfection from that attribute, and the one who is perfect through another is deficient in himself, and the one in need of another is also deficient in himself—and that is impossible for God. As for the talk of perfection and deficiency, it is rhetorical, and it is opposed by what was established in the beginning: that the attribute of knowledge is a perfection and ignorance is a deficiency, and God is exalted above deficiency. And the second opposition: that knowledge is a perfection, and it is not possible for God to derive perfection from other than Him. So the answer that it is rhetorical is not valid, nor does it refute it by saying that knowledge is perfection and ignorance is deficiency and God is exalted above deficiency, because the objector says: God is also exalted above acquiring perfection from other than Him, and negating acquisition from God is not rhetorical. The answer: deficient essences derive perfection from their perfect attributes; as for perfect essences, their attributes are only perfect because they are attributes of those essences, and the perfection of knowledge is of this type, for the cause of its perfection is its being an attribute of God.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the second sub-objection (الثانى) and its response. The objector argues: if knowledge is a perfection, God's possessing it would mean He acquires perfection from it, implying He is perfect through another, which makes Him deficient in Himself—impossible for God. Razi initially dismisses this as rhetorical, citing an established principle that knowledge is perfection and ignorance deficiency. But then a stronger counter-objection is raised: the objector can agree with that but still insist that acquiring perfection from an attribute entails dependence on another, which is impossible for God. The final answer distinguishes between deficient essences (ذوات ناقصة) which need attributes to perfect themselves, and perfect essences (ذوات كاملة) like God, whose attributes are perfect precisely because they are attributes of the perfect essence. The perfection of knowledge is not a separate acquired perfection but inherent due to its being God's attribute.

Key Terms
صفة كمال
ناقص بذاته
ذوات ناقصة
ذوات كاملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any attribute of perfection must be a separate perfection acquired by the essence, implying dependence.
Correction: Razi's distinction shows that for a perfect essence (God), attributes are perfect because they are attributes of that essence, not because they confer perfection upon it.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's final answer, why does God's knowledge not imply dependence?
A) Because knowledge is not actually a perfection.
B) Because God's essence is perfect and its attributes are perfect by being attributes of that perfect essence.
C) Because God acquires perfection from Himself.
D) Because the objection is merely rhetorical.

Key Takeaway
The perfection of God's attributes is not acquired; it is inherent because the attributes belong to a perfect essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is absolutely perfect and does not acquire perfection from His attributes, what does that mean for your own striving for perfection? Are you trying to make yourself 'complete' through your achievements, or are you content with being a deficient essence that relies entirely on God's grace?
?
Consider the attributes you value in yourself—intelligence, kindness, strength. Do you see them as qualities that make you 'more' on your own, or as gifts from the Perfect One that derive their goodness from Him? How does shifting that perspective change your humility and gratitude?
?
When you feel deficient or failing, do you believe that this deficiency somehow diminishes God's plan or your worth in His sight? Or can you embrace your neediness as a creature, knowing that only God is perfect in essence, and that your very dependence is the path to Him?
