Why Power Alone Cannot Suffice: The Infinite Regress Argument
Razi defends will as distinct from power against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the necessity of will as a distinct attribute by showing that power alone leads to infinite regress and that a self-determined will would undermine divine freedom.

Core Idea
If will were self-determined, God would be a necessary agent, not free.

In this passage, Razi responds to two objections against the need for will as a specificator. First, he shows that power alone cannot specify because its relation is equal—and the same problem applies to will, leading to an infinite regress. Second, he argues that if will had an inherent quality determining its attachment, that would make God a necessitated agent (mujib bi-dh-dhat), which contradicts the Islamic conception of divine free choice. The argument preserves the Ash'ari commitment to ikhtiyar.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic source and its literal translation carefully. Notice the dialectical structure: objection, response, modified objection, final refutation.
The passage is a continuous argument; follow the back-and-forth.
2
Identify the first objection and Razi's response
The objector says power suffices since will's relation is also equal. Razi points out that if will is needed because of equal relation, then will itself would need another will ad infinitum.
This shows the infinite regress (tasalsul) is absurd.
3
Examine the modified objection
The objector then claims that the eternal will has a specific quality that determines its attachment to a particular time, avoiding regress.
4
Understand Razi's counter: necessitarianism
Razi says if will's attachment is necessary due to its own quality, then God would be a necessitated agent (mujib bi-dh-dhat), not a free agent (mukhtar). This is the view of the philosophers, which Ash'aris reject.
This is a key theological point: divine freedom requires will to be free, not determined.
5
See the final argument: power could also have a quality
Razi adds that if will can have such a quality, then power could also have a similar quality, making will superfluous. So the objector's move fails to eliminate the need for will.
This shows the inconsistency in the objector's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنه لا بد من مخصص فلم لا يكفى القدرة . قوله : نسبتها إلى الكل على السواء. قلنا : والإرادة أيضا نسبتها إلى الكل على السواء فلتفتقر الإرادة إلى إرادة أخرى لا إلى نهاية . فإن قلت : الإرادة القديمة كانت على صفة لاجلها يجب تعلقها بإحداث الحادث المعين في الوقت المعين ويستحيل تعلقها بإحداث ذلك الحادث فى وقت آخر . قلت : لو كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن الله تعالى بالحقيقة مختارا بل كان موجبا بالذات وهو قول الفلاسفة، وأيضاً فإن جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال قدرة الله كانت على صفة لأجلها يجب تعلقها بإيجاد الحادث المعين في الوقت المعين ويستحيل تعلقها بإيجاده فى وقت آخر، وعلى هذا التقدير تستغنى القدرة عن الإرادة
We grant that a specificator is necessary, but why is power not sufficient? You say: Its relation to all times is equal. We say: Will also has its relation to all times equal, so will would need another will without end. Then you say: The eternal will had a quality due to which its attachment to bringing about the specific event at the specific time is necessary, and its attachment to bringing about that event at another time is impossible. I say: If that were the case, God would not truly be a free agent (mukhtar), but rather a necessitated agent (mujib bi-dh-dhat), which is the position of the philosophers. Also, if that is permissible, why is it not permissible to say that God's power had a quality due to which its attachment to bringing about the specific event at the specific time is necessary, and its attachment to bringing about it at another time is impossible? On this supposition, power would be independent of will.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage continues the defense of will as a distinct attribute. The objector argues that power alone might be the specificator. Razi refutes by noting that power's relation to all times is equal, but then the same applies to will — so will would need another will ad infinitum, which is absurd. The objector then modifies the claim: the eternal will has a specific quality that determines its attachment to a particular time. Razi responds that this would make God a necessary agent (mujib bi-dh-dhat) rather than a free agent (mukhtar), which is the view of the philosophers. Furthermore, if will can have such a determining quality, then power could equally have it, making will unnecessary. This argument preserves the Ash'ari commitment to divine freedom (ikhtiyar) and the distinction between will and power.

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
تسلسل
موجب بالذات
مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if will is self-determining (has a quality that dictates its attachment), then it avoids the regress and God remains free.
Correction: Razi shows that such self-determination actually removes freedom because the will's attachment becomes necessary, turning God into a necessitated agent (mujib bi-dh-dhat). True freedom requires that will be able to choose otherwise, not be determined by its own nature.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Razi draw if the eternal will has a quality that determines its attachment to a specific time?
A) God would be truly free
B) God would be a necessitated agent (mujib bi-dh-dhat)
C) Will would need another will
D) Power would become unnecessary

Key Takeaway
Razi defends divine freedom by showing that assuming will's self-determination leads to necessitarianism, thus preserving the need for will as a distinct and free attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's will is absolutely free and not necessitated by any quality, how would that change the way you pray for something seemingly impossible? Will you now pray with more certainty that He can choose to respond differently than you expect?
?
Razi warns against making God's will necessary. Do you ever secretly assume that God 'must' answer your dua in a certain way because you are righteous or because the situation demands it? Confess that assumption and ask Him to remind you of His absolute freedom.
?
When you face a major decision, do you actually believe that God is free to will any outcome, or do you act as if His will is predictable? What one step can you take today to live in light of God's unfettered freedom?

The Objection that Knowledge Suffices for Specification
Razi presents two arguments that divine knowledge alone is sufficient for specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments for why divine knowledge alone might be sufficient to explain the specification of creation, without needing divine will.

Core Idea
Knowledge of benefit and harm can motivate action, so why would God need will?

In this passage, Razi presents an objection from an opponent who argues that God's omniscience makes His will redundant. The objector claims that knowledge of what is beneficial or harmful is itself enough to motivate action, and moreover, God's knowledge of what will occur determines everything, leaving no role for will. Let's examine these two arguments carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two arguments numbered by the author.
The passage begins with 'سلمنا أن القدرة' and ends with 'فكان ذلك كافيا في التخصيص'.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find 'أحدهما' and 'الثاني'. The first uses the example of Hell to show knowledge outweighs will. The second relies on divine foreknowledge determining existence.
The first argument appeals to human experience; the second is logical.
3
Reflect on the force of the objection
Consider why this objection is challenging. If knowledge alone suffices, what role remains for divine will? Think about how the objector uses this to deny the need for will.
This sets up Razi's later response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن القدرة غير صالحة لذلك فلم لا يكفى العلم . بيانه من وجهين . أحدهما : أن الله تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات فيكون عالما بما فيها من المصالح والمفاسد ، والعلم باشتمال الفعل على المصلحة والمفسدة مستقل بالدعاء إلى الإيجاد والترك بدليل أنا متى علمنا في الفعل مصلحة خالية عن المضار دعانا ذلك العلم إلى العمل ، بل إسناد الترجيح إلى هذا الفعل أولى من إسناده إلى الإرادة ، فإن الله تعالى أوقف على شفير جهنم وخلق فيه علما بما في دخول النار من المضار وخلق منهم إرادة وصول النار فلا يدخل النار فلأجل ذلك قد نريد الشيء إرادة قوية ونتركه لعلمنا بما فيه من المفسدة . الثاني : وهو أن الله تعالى عالم بجميع الأشياء فيعلم أن أيها يقع وأيها لا يقع ووجود ما علم الله تعالى عدمه محال ، وبالعكس فلا جرم يوجد ما علم وجوده فكان ذلك كافيا في التخصيص . سلمنا أن ما ذكرته يدل على ذلك لكن معنا ما يبطله وهو أن المريد إما أن يريد لغرض أو لا لغرض ، فان كان لغرض كان مستكملا بذلك الغرض والمستكمل بالغير ناقص بالذات وهو على الله تعالى محال ، وإذا كان لا لغرض كان ذلك عبثا وهو على الله تعالى محال ، ولأنه يقتضى ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح وهو محال . والجواب : أن الجسم الموصوف بالحركة كان يمكن أن يصير موصوفا بها قبل ذلك ، والمحكوم عليه بهذا الإمكان ليس هو المعدوم بل هو الجسم الموجود
We grant that power is not suitable for that, so why is knowledge not sufficient? Its explanation is from two aspects. One: God, exalted, knows all known things, so He knows what is in them of benefits and harms. Knowledge of an act's comprising benefit or harm is independent in calling to action or abandonment, as evidenced by the fact that whenever we know in an act a benefit free from harm, that knowledge calls us to action. Rather, attributing preference to this act is more appropriate than attributing it to will. Indeed, God stops [a person] at the edge of Hell and creates in him knowledge of the harms of entering the Fire, and creates in him an will to reach the Fire, yet he does not enter the Fire. For that reason, we may will something with a strong will and yet abandon it due to our knowledge of the harm in it. The second: God knows all things, so He knows which will occur and which will not. The existence of what God knows to be non-existent is impossible, and conversely. So necessarily what He knows to exist exists, and that suffices for specification. We grant that what you mentioned indicates that, but we have something that refutes it: the willing one either wills for a purpose or not. If for a purpose, then He is perfected by that purpose, and one who is perfected by another is deficient in essence, and that is impossible for God. If not for a purpose, then that is absurd, and that is impossible for God. And because it necessitates preferring one side of the possible over the other without a preferrer, which is impossible. The answer: The body described with motion could have become described with it before that, and the subject of this possibility is not the non-existent but rather the existing body.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents two objections and the beginning of a response. First, the objector argues that knowledge alone suffices for specification: (1) knowledge of benefit/harm is itself motivating, as seen in human experience where knowledge outweighs will; (2) God's knowledge determines what occurs, so whatever He knows to exist must exist, making will superfluous. Then the objector introduces a separate argument against will: if God wills for a purpose, He would be perfected by that purpose (imperfection), and if without purpose, it is absurd (عبث) and also requires a preferrer without a reason, which is impossible. The response (beginning here) redirects: the possibility is attributed to an existing body, not to a non-existent one, thus the problem of preferring non-existents is avoided. The argument continues beyond this snippet.

Key Terms
علم
مصلحة
مفسدة
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God knows everything, His will is unnecessary because knowledge alone determines action.
Correction: Razi will later argue that knowledge does not bring about existence; will is the attribute that specifies and creates, while knowledge is merely awareness.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi give to show that knowledge of harm can override a strong will?
A) A person knowing a food is poisonous but still eating it
B) A person standing at the edge of Hell, knowing its harm, yet not entering despite willing to do so
C) A child wanting to touch fire despite knowing it burns
D) A person choosing a beneficial medicine despite hating it

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that divine knowledge suffices for specification, citing its motivational power and determinism, challenging the necessity of divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge of harm can override your strongest desires, why do you still choose to sin when you know its harm? What does that reveal about your will?
?
The objector argues that God's knowledge alone suffices for specification. Do you sometimes act as if your own knowledge of right and wrong is enough without aligning your will to God's command? How can you cultivate a will that submits?
?
Consider a time when you knew something was harmful but did it anyway. What was stronger than your knowledge? What does that teach you about the role of will in your relationship with Allah?

The Purpose/Absurdity Objection and the Response
Razi presents the purpose/absurdity objection against divine will and begins his response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine will either implies a purpose (imperfection) or absurdity, and the beginning of the response that possibility qualifies existing bodies, not non-existents.

Core Idea
Willing without purpose is absurd; but God's will is not purposeless.

Having set aside the knowledge objection, Razi presents another challenge: divine will itself seems problematic. If God wills for a purpose, He would be perfected by that purpose, implying deficiency. If He wills without purpose, that is absurd. Razi begins his response by reinterpreting the subject of possibility, shifting it from non-existents to existing bodies. This lesson explores this objection and the initial counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage starting from 'سلمنا أن ما ذكرته' up to 'عبثا وهو على الله تعالى محال'. Understand the dilemma: either God wills for a purpose (implying imperfection) or without purpose (absurd).
The objector adds that without purpose, preference without a preferrer is also impossible.
2
Examine the response
Read the answer: 'والجواب'. Note how Razi shifts the discussion to possibility (إمكان) and says it is attributed to an existing body, not a non-existent entity.
The response is partial; it counters the idea that preference without a preferrer applies to non-existents.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how reattributing possibility to existing bodies avoids the problem of preferring one non-existent over another. This is a key move in the debate.
Full response continues beyond this snippet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception: Some think God's will must be for a purpose like human will, leading to the conclusion that God is imperfect if He wills for a purpose.

Checkpoint
What is one of the horns of the dilemma presented against divine will?
A) If God wills for a purpose, He is imperfect because He needs that purpose to perfect Him.
B) If God wills for a purpose, He is all-powerful.
C) If God wills without purpose, He is free.
D) If God wills without purpose, His will is identical to His knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The objector's dilemma against divine will is answered by Razi's shift of possibility to existing bodies, avoiding the absurdity of purposelessness.

?

?
If God's will is not driven by need or lack, then what does it mean for you to trust His decrees when they seem pointless or painful? Can you honestly name one situation in your life right now where you are tempted to accuse God of 'عبث' (absurdity) because you cannot see the purpose?
?
Razi shifts the subject of possibility from the non-existent to the existing. In your spiritual life, are you more focused on possibilities that do not exist (regretting the past, fearing the future) rather than what actually exists before you, which is the real arena of God's will and your responsibility?

The Problem of Will and Infinite Regress
Razi presents and defends an infinite regress argument against will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing the specification of events to divine will leads to an infinite regress, and how an objection based on the eternal will's specific quality is answered.

Core Idea
Will cannot be the ultimate specifier because it would need another will forever.

This passage presents a critical argument against the sufficiency of divine will as the ground for specifying which events occur. Imam Razi shows that if will is the decider, it itself requires another will to decide its own relation to possibilities, leading to infinite regress. He then addresses an objection and extends the argument to divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Focus on the flow of the argument: statement, objection, response, extension.
The OCR has a word 'السكل' which likely means 'الكل'.
2
Identify the infinite regress argument
Notice how will's equal relation to all things means it cannot specify without another will. This is the core point.
3
Understand the objection
The objector claims the eternal will has a quality that ties it to specific events. See how this would solve regress.
4
Analyze the response
The response shows the objection leads to God being necessitated, not free. Also extends the logic to power.
Note how the extension mirrors the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : والإرادة أيضا نسبتها إلى السكل على السواء فلتفتقر الإرادة إلى إرادة أخرى لا إلى نهاية . فإن قلت : الإرادة القديمة كانت على صفة لاجلها يجب تعلقها بأحداث الحادث المعين في الوقت المعين ويستحيل تعلقها بإحداث ذلك الحادث فى وقت آخر . قلت : لو كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن الله تعالى بالحقيقة مختارا بل كان موجبا بالذات وهو قول الفلاسفة، وأيضاً فإن جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال قدرة الله كانت على صفة الأجلها يجب تعلقها بإيجاد الحادث المعين في الوقت المعين ويستحيل تعلقها بإيجاده فى وقت آخر، وعلى هذا التقدير تستغنى القدرة عن الإرادة ، سلمنا أن القدرة غير صالحة لذلك فلم لا يكفى العلم . بيانه من وجهين . أحدهما : أن الله تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات فيكون عالما بما فيها من المصالح والمفاسد ، والعلم باشتمال الفعل على المصلحة والمفسدة مستقل بالدعاء إلى الإيجاد والترك بدليل أنا متى علمنا في الفعل مصلحة خالية عن المضار دعانا ذلك العلم إلى العمل ، بل إسناد الترجيح إلى هذا الفعل أولى من إسناده إلى الإرادة ، فإن الله تعالى أوقف على شفير جهنم وخلق فيه علما بما في دخول النار من المضار وخلق منهم إرادة وصول النار فلايدخل النار فلأجل ذلك قد نريد الشيء إرادة قوية ونتركه لعلمنا بما فيه من المفسدة . الثاني : وهو أن الله تعالى عالم بجميع الأشياء فيعلم أن أيها يقع وأيها لا يقع ووجود ما علم الله تعالى عدمه محال ، وبالعكس فلا جرم يوجد ما علم وجوده فكان ذلك كافيا في التخصيص
We said: The will also, its relation to [؟] is equal, so the will would need another will ad infinitum. For if you say: The eternal will was such that it must be attached to the happening of the specific event at the specific time, and it is impossible for it to be attached to causing that event at another time. I said: If that were the case, then God Almighty would not truly be a chooser, but rather necessitated by essence, and that is the statement of the philosophers. Also, if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible to say that the power of God was such that it must be attached to bringing about the specific event at the specific time, and it is impossible for it to be attached to bringing it about at another time; and on this assumption, power would be independent of will. We concede that power is not suitable for that, so why is knowledge not sufficient? Its explanation is from two aspects. The first: That God Almighty knows all known things, so He knows what is in them of benefits and harms. And knowledge that an action contains benefit or harm is independently sufficient to call for doing or abandoning, as evidenced by the fact that when we know in an action a benefit free from harms, that knowledge calls us to action. Rather, attributing preponderance to this action is more appropriate than attributing it to will. For God Almighty stopped [someone] on the edge of Hell and created in him knowledge of the harms of entering the Fire, and created in them a will to reach the Fire, yet he does not enter the Fire. Therefore, we may will something strongly and leave it due to our knowledge of what harm is in it. The second: That God Almighty knows all things, so He knows which of them will occur and which will not occur. The existence of what God knows to be non-existent is impossible, and vice versa, so necessarily what He knows to exist does exist, and that is sufficient for specification.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical argument about divine will. It begins with the author's claim that will (al-irada) needs another will ad infinitum because its relation to all things is equal. Then an objector (فإن قلت) raises a point: the eternal will is such that it necessarily attaches to a specific event at a specific time, so it doesn't need another will. The author (قلت) responds that if the eternal will were necessarily determinate, God would not be truly free (mukhtar) but necessitated (mujib bi al-dhat), which is the philosophers' view. He then extends the objection to power (al-qudra): if the objector's reasoning is accepted, then power could also be determinate and thus independent of will. The author concedes that power might not be suitable (for such determination), but then asks: why not knowledge (al-ilm)? He then proceeds to give two proofs (بيانه من وجهين) that knowledge is sufficient for specifying acts. The first proof: God knows all things, including the benefits (masalih) and harms (mafasid) in actions. Knowledge of benefit/harm alone is sufficient to motivate action or abandonment, as seen in human experience—we often refrain from something we strongly will because we know its harm. The second proof: God knows which things will occur and which will not; what He knows to occur must occur, and what He knows not to occur cannot occur. This knowledge alone suffices to determine what happens, without requiring a separate will.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الموجب بالذات
المختار
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine will is self-sufficient and needs no explanation for its specification.
Correction: The passage shows that will, if considered the specificer, would itself require another will, leading to infinite regress. Thus it cannot be the ultimate basis.

Checkpoint
According to the initial argument, why does will need another will?
A) Because will is weak and needs support.
B) Because will's relation to all possibilities is equal, so it cannot specify without a further will.
C) Because God's will is eternal and unchanging.
D) Because the objector claims it does.

Key Takeaway
Will cannot be the ultimate specifier because it would require an infinite chain of wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hesitate in making a decision, do you ever feel you need 'another will' to specify your choice? How does Razi's argument about the ultimate specifier challenge you to break that cycle and trust in God's absolute will as the final decider?
?
If you believe that God's will has a special quality that necessitates His actions, are you secretly reducing God to a predictable machine? Examine your heart: do you truly submit to a God who is absolutely free, or do you wish He were controllable like a necessitated agent?
?
The argument extends to power: if any attribute could be self-specifying, we would not need multiple attributes. In your own life, do you rely on your own attributes (like willpower or intelligence) as ultimate specifiers, forgetting that all specification comes from God alone? How can you actively shift your reliance to Him today?

Two Proofs That Knowledge Suffices for Specification
Razi presents two proofs that knowledge alone suffices for specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs Imam Razi offers to show that God's knowledge alone is sufficient to specify which events occur, without needing a separate will.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of benefits, harms, and future events is enough to determine what happens.

After arguing that will and power lead to problems, Imam Razi turns to knowledge. He concedes that power is not suitable, but asks why knowledge would not be sufficient. He then gives two proofs: one from human experience that knowledge of benefit/harm motivates action, and another from God's foreknowledge necessitating occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the Arabic passage starting from 'سلمنا أن القدرة غير صالحة لذلك' to the end. Follow the two proofs.
2
Analyze the first proof
Understand how knowledge of benefit or harm is sufficient to call to action or abandonment. Note the example of being stopped on the edge of Hell.
This proof relies on human experience (shahid).
3
Analyze the second proof
God's knowledge of what will occur and what will not makes occurrence and non-occurrence necessary. This is a logical argument.
4
Reflect on the sufficiency argument
Consider how these proofs show that knowledge can take the role of will in specification, without infinite regress.
This is the conclusion of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : والإرادة أيضا نسبتها إلى السكل على السواء فلتفتقر الإرادة إلى إرادة أخرى لا إلى نهاية . فإن قلت : الإرادة القديمة كانت على صفة لاجلها يجب تعلقها بأحداث الحادث المعين في الوقت المعين ويستحيل تعلقها بإحداث ذلك الحادث فى وقت آخر . قلت : لو كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن الله تعالى بالحقيقة مختارا بل كان موجبا بالذات وهو قول الفلاسفة، وأيضاً فإن جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال قدرة الله كانت على صفة الأجلها يجب تعلقها بإيجاد الحادث المعين في الوقت المعين ويستحيل تعلقها بإيجاده فى وقت آخر، وعلى هذا التقدير تستغنى القدرة عن الإرادة ، سلمنا أن القدرة غير صالحة لذلك فلم لا يكفى العلم . بيانه من وجهين . أحدهما : أن الله تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات فيكون عالما بما فيها من المصالح والمفاسد ، والعلم باشتمال الفعل على المصلحة والمفسدة مستقل بالدعاء إلى الإيجاد والترك بدليل أنا متى علمنا في الفعل مصلحة خالية عن المضار دعانا ذلك العلم إلى العمل ، بل إسناد الترجيح إلى هذا الفعل أولى من إسناده إلى الإرادة ، فإن الله تعالى أوقف على شفير جهنم وخلق فيه علما بما في دخول النار من المضار وخلق منهم إرادة وصول النار فلايدخل النار فلأجل ذلك قد نريد الشيء إرادة قوية ونتركه لعلمنا بما فيه من المفسدة . الثاني : وهو أن الله تعالى عالم بجميع الأشياء فيعلم أن أيها يقع وأيها لا يقع ووجود ما علم الله تعالى عدمه محال ، وبالعكس فلا جرم يوجد ما علم وجوده فكان ذلك كافيا في التخصيص
We said: The will also, its relation to [؟] is equal, so the will would need another will ad infinitum. For if you say: The eternal will was such that it must be attached to the happening of the specific event at the specific time, and it is impossible for it to be attached to causing that event at another time. I said: If that were the case, then God Almighty would not truly be a chooser, but rather necessitated by essence, and that is the statement of the philosophers. Also, if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible to say that the power of God was such that it must be attached to bringing about the specific event at the specific time, and it is impossible for it to be attached to bringing it about at another time; and on this assumption, power would be independent of will. We concede that power is not suitable for that, so why is knowledge not sufficient? Its explanation is from two aspects. The first: That God Almighty knows all known things, so He knows what is in them of benefits and harms. And knowledge that an action contains benefit or harm is independently sufficient to call for doing or abandoning, as evidenced by the fact that when we know in an action a benefit free from harms, that knowledge calls us to action. Rather, attributing preponderance to this action is more appropriate than attributing it to will. For God Almighty stopped [someone] on the edge of Hell and created in him knowledge of the harms of entering the Fire, and created in them a will to reach the Fire, yet he does not enter the Fire. Therefore, we may will something strongly and leave it due to our knowledge of what harm is in it. The second: That God Almighty knows all things, so He knows which of them will occur and which will not occur. The existence of what God knows to be non-existent is impossible, and vice versa, so necessarily what He knows to exist does exist, and that is sufficient for specification.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical argument about divine will. It begins with the author's claim that will (al-irada) needs another will ad infinitum because its relation to all things is equal. Then an objector (فإن قلت) raises a point: the eternal will is such that it necessarily attaches to a specific event at a specific time, so it doesn't need another will. The author (قلت) responds that if the eternal will were necessarily determinate, God would not be truly free (mukhtar) but necessitated (mujib bi al-dhat), which is the philosophers' view. He then extends the objection to power (al-qudra): if the objector's reasoning is accepted, then power could also be determinate and thus independent of will. The author concedes that power might not be suitable (for such determination), but then asks: why not knowledge (al-ilm)? He then proceeds to give two proofs (بيانه من وجهين) that knowledge is sufficient for specifying acts. The first proof: God knows all things, including the benefits (masalih) and harms (mafasid) in actions. Knowledge of benefit/harm alone is sufficient to motivate action or abandonment, as seen in human experience—we often refrain from something we strongly will because we know its harm. The second proof: God knows which things will occur and which will not; what He knows to occur must occur, and what He knows not to occur cannot occur. This knowledge alone suffices to determine what happens, without requiring a separate will.

Key Terms
العلم
المصالح
المفاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge is passive and cannot cause action or specification.
Correction: The first proof shows that knowledge of benefit/harm actively calls to action, and the second shows that foreknowledge necessitates events.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that knowledge suffices for specification?
A) Knowledge of benefit or harm is enough to call to action or abandonment.
B) Knowledge is eternal and unchanging.
C) Will always follows knowledge.
D) Knowledge is an attribute of perfection.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge alone, through its understanding of good and evil and its foreknowledge, is sufficient to specify all events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that a certain habit harms your relationship with God, yet you continue it. What does this lesson reveal about the gap between your knowledge and your actions, and what concrete step will you take today to let your knowledge override your will?
?
God's knowledge of your future is infallible. If He knows you will commit a sin, does that make you blameless? How does the second proof in this lesson challenge you to take full responsibility for your choices despite divine foreknowledge?
?
In the example, knowledge of harm was enough to stop someone from entering Hell. Where in your life is your will driving you toward something destructive, and how can you use your knowledge of the harm to resist, trusting that God's knowledge is sufficient to guide you?

God’s Will Transcends Purpose
Objection and answer on divine will's purpose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's will does not require a purpose or external reason, but rather attaches to its object necessarily by its own essence.

Core Idea
God's will is self-sufficient: it needs no purpose.

This lesson tackles a classic objection against divine will: if God's will acts for a purpose, it seems He lacks something; if without purpose, it seems arbitrary. The answer reveals that God's will is not like human will—it is not motivated by external goals but rather attaches to its object necessarily by its own essence. Let's explore how this objection is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection: 'The willer either gives preponderance for a purpose or not for a purpose.' Understand the dilemma presented.
2
Understand the dilemma's force
Realize why both horns are problematic: if for a purpose, God would be deficient (seeking completion); if not for a purpose, the act would be arbitrary (preference without reason), which is absurd.
3
Examine the answer's key phrase
Read the answer: 'The will of God is exalted above accidental purposes; rather, it is necessary for it to attach to bringing that into existence at that time by its essence.' Notice the claim that will attaches 'by its essence' (li dhatiha).
4
Grasp the concept of necessary attachment
Understand that for God, the will does not need an external purpose because its very essence necessitates a specific object and time. This avoids both deficiency and arbitrariness.
5
Connect to divine transcendence
Reflect on how this teaching safeguards God's perfection: His will is not subject to the limitations of human volition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : المريد إما أن يرجح لغرض أو لا لغرض . قلنا : إرادة الله تعالى منزهة عن الأعراض بل هى واجب به التعلق بإيجاد ذلك في ذلك الوقت لذاتها
He said: The willer either gives preponderance for a purpose or not for a purpose. We said: The will of God Almighty is exalted above accidental purposes; rather, it is necessary for it to attach to bringing that into existence at that time by its essence.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector presents a dilemma: the willer (al-murid) must give preponderance either for a purpose (gharad) or without one. If for a purpose, that purpose implies deficiency in God (since He would be seeking completion). If without purpose, the act is absurd (abath) and amounts to preferring one possibility over another without a preponderant (murajjih), which is impossible. The answer (قلنا) denies both horns: God's will is free from accidental goals (a'rad); rather, it is necessary (wajib) for it to attach to bringing about that specific event at that specific time by its very essence (li dhatiha). Thus, no external purpose is needed, and the will is not arbitrary but inherently determinate.

Key Terms
المريد
الغرض
الإرادة
الذات
الأعراض
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will must have a reason or purpose like human will.
Correction: God's will attaches to its object by its own essence, not for any external purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is God's will not subject to the dilemma of having a purpose or being absurd?
A) Because God's will is eternal and cannot change.
B) Because God's will attaches necessarily by its essence without need for purpose.
C) Because God's will is identical to His knowledge.
D) Because God's will is an accident.

Key Takeaway
God's will is not driven by external purposes but necessarily attaches to its object by its own essence, preserving divine perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a tragedy strikes and you cannot see any good reason, do you secretly resent God for being arbitrary? How does knowing that His will is self-sufficient—not needing a purpose you can grasp—challenge you to trust His wisdom even when it seems pointless?
?
Do you often find yourself trying to justify God's commands to yourself before obeying, as if He needs your approval? How would it change your obedience to truly believe that His will is perfect and needs no external justification?
?
If God's will is not driven by any need or purpose, what does that say about your own tendency to serve God only when you feel you get something in return? Are you willing to worship Him purely because He is worthy, without expecting any benefit?

4 min
Knowledge Follows, Not Causes, Existence
Objection and answer about knowledge and existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge alone cannot determine existence and why another attribute (will) is necessary to avoid logical circularity.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows reality; it does not create it.

Some argue that God's knowledge directly determines what exists. But this leads to a logical problem: if knowledge caused existence, then the thing's existence would depend on knowledge, but knowledge of that existence would depend on the thing's existence — a circularity. In this passage, Imam al-Razi clarifies the correct order: knowledge is consequent upon a thing's being destined to exist, which itself requires another divine attribute, namely will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening claim: 'Only that exists which God knows will exist.' This seems to make knowledge the cause of existence.
The objector assumes that knowledge precedes and determines existence.
2
Analyze the response
The author responds that knowledge of a thing's future existence is consequent (tabi‘) upon the thing's being such that it will exist — not the other way around.
Knowledge is a perfect reflection of reality, not its creator.
3
Identify the circularity and the need for another attribute
If the state of 'being such that it will exist' were caused by knowledge, a circularity (dawr) would result. Therefore, another attribute — divine will — must make the thing such that it will exist, and knowledge follows accordingly.
This preserves the logical order: will → existence → knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : إنما يوجد ما علم الله تعالى أنه يوجد . قلنا : العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد تابع لكونه بحيث سيوجد فكونه بحيث سيوجد لوكان لأجل ذلك العلم لوم الدور بل لابد من صفة أخرى .
He said: Only that exists which God knows will exist. We said: Knowledge that a thing will exist is consequent upon its being such that it will exist; if its being such that it will exist were because of that knowledge, circularity would occur; rather, there must be another attribute.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and answer. The objector argues that what God knows to exist is what actually exists, so knowledge alone determines existence. The author responds that knowledge (al-ilm) that something will exist is dependent on (tabi') the thing's being such that it will exist (kawnuhu bihaythu sayujad). If the thing's state of being destined to exist were caused by that very knowledge, then circularity (al-dawr) would result: the knowledge would cause the state, and the state would cause the knowledge. Therefore, there must be another attribute (sifa ukhra) — namely, will (al-irada) — that makes the thing such that it will exist, and then knowledge follows accordingly.

Key Terms
العلم
الوجود
التابع
الدور
الصفة الأخرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge directly causes things to exist.
Correction: God's knowledge is perfect and corresponds to what will exist, but it is not the cause; rather, His will determines what will exist, and knowledge follows that determination.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that another attribute is needed beyond knowledge?
A) Because knowledge cannot cause existence without circularity.
B) Because knowledge is eternal.
C) Because God's will is stronger than knowledge.
D) Because knowledge is dependent on will.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is consequent upon the thing's being destined to exist, which requires an act of will to avoid circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes assume that because God knows everything that will happen, He approves of your every action? How does this lesson's distinction between knowledge and will challenge that comfort?
?
Have you ever felt trapped by God's foreknowledge, as if your choices don't matter? How does the argument that knowledge follows the thing's state (and that state is caused by will) restore your sense of responsibility?
?
The refutation avoids circularity by positing another attribute (will). In your life, do you ever try to avoid taking responsibility by appealing to God's knowledge? How can you instead see your actions as part of what God wills for you, and act accordingly?

Knowledge and Will: Avoiding Circularity
The author responds to an objection about knowledge and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge cannot be the cause of a thing's existence and how divine will operates without external purpose.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows reality; will precedes existence without purpose.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if God's knowledge of a future thing caused that thing's existence, we would have circular reasoning. The author clarifies that knowledge is consequent upon the thing's destined state, and another attribute—will—accounts for that state. He also defends divine will from being motivated by external purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: a statement, then an objection, then a response.
2
Analyze the circularity argument
The author first asserts that knowledge of future existence is consequent upon the thing's destined state. If that state were caused by knowledge, it would be circular. Therefore, another attribute (will) must account for it.
Circularity (دور) means A depends on B and B depends on A, which is logically invalid.
3
Understand the response about divine will
An objector raises the issue of purpose: either a willer chooses for a purpose or not. The author replies that Allah's will is free from purposes and necessarily attaches to creating at the specific time by its own essence.
This defends divine transcendence from anthropomorphic notions of motivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد تابع لكونه بحيث سيوجد فكونه بحيث سيوجد لو كان لأجل ذلك العلم لزم الدور بل لابد من صفة أخرى . قوله : المريد إما أن يرجح لغرض أو لا لغرض . قلنا : إرادة الله تعالى منزهة عن الأعراض بل هي واجبة التعلق بإيجاد ذلك في ذلك الوقت لذاتها
We said: Knowledge that a thing will exist is consequent upon its being such that it will exist; so if its being such that it will exist were because of that knowledge, there would be circularity, but rather there must be another attribute. His statement: The one who wills either gives preponderance for a purpose or not for a purpose. We said: The will of Allah is exalted above purposes, rather it is necessarily attached to bringing that thing into existence at that time by its essence.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer in a dialectical argument about divine will. The author first states that knowledge of a thing's future existence depends on the thing's objective state of being destined to exist. If that destined state were itself caused by the knowledge, there would be circularity. Therefore, another attribute (such as will) must account for that state. Then an objector says that the one who wills either chooses for a purpose or without purpose. The author responds that Allah's will is free from purposes (gharad) and necessarily attaches to creating at a specific time by its own essence, not due to any external motive.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge causes the existence of the known object.
Correction: The author clarifies that knowledge is consequent upon the thing's destined state; if it were causal, circularity would result.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if a thing's destined-to-exist state were caused by knowledge of it?
A) It would be impossible
B) There would be circularity
C) It would require another will
D) It would be eternal

Key Takeaway
Knowledge follows the actual state of affairs; divine will determines existence without external purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Scope of the Proof of Divine Will
Commentator limits the proof of will to temporal acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope and limitation of the classical proof for the divine will, and why it only directly applies to temporal actions.

Core Idea
The proof of will covers only events in time, not timeless acts like creation itself.

The commentator here points out a subtle but crucial nuance in the argument for God's will. The proof provided earlier in the text works for actions that happen at specific moments, but the creation of time and the universe are not such events. This observation forces us to either find another proof or reconsider the necessity of will for those foundational acts. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the clear distinction between 'actions occurring in times' and those that are not.
2
Identify the two categories of actions
The passage divides acts into two groups: temporal actions (occurring in time) and non-temporal actions (like creating time, body, and causes of time). Note the examples given.
3
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator argues that the earlier proof only works for the first category. For the second, either a new proof is needed, or one must accept that those acts occur without will—a position the opponents do not hold.
This shows the proof is not universal.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this limitation matters. If the proof of will is incomplete, then the attribute of will might require additional justification for foundational divine acts.
This connects to the broader kalam debate on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : الحجة التي أوردها على إثبات الإرادة خاصة بأفعال تقع في أزمنة أما التي لا تكون واقعة في أزمنة مثل خلق الزمان والجسم وسائر علل الزمان إن كانت بإرادة احتيج في إثبات الإرادة هناك إلى حجة أخرى ، إلا أن يقال أنها تحصل من غير إرادة وذلك ما لم يقولوا به
(1) I say: The proof he adduced for establishing will is specific to actions occurring in times. As for those that do not occur in time, such as the creation of time, body, and all causes of time, if they are by will, then establishing will for them requires another proof, unless it is said that they occur without will, and that is something they did not say.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the author's proof of will. The commentator points out that the proof only works for actions that happen at specific times (temporal actions). For actions that are not in time, like creating time or bodies themselves, the same proof cannot apply. If one claims those also require will, another argument is needed. The exception would be if one claimed those occur without will, but the author's opponents do not hold that view.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of divine will is universal and covers all divine acts.

Checkpoint

A) Miracles
B) Temporal actions
C) Creation of time and body
D) Human actions

Key Takeaway
The classical proof of will is limited to temporal acts; timeless creation requires a separate argument or a different approach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Does that idea unsettle you? How do you reconcile your understanding of God's attributes with the logical limitations of human proofs?

Universal Proof of Will via Specification
A proof and criticism of divine will via specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal proof of divine will based on the need for a specifier, and why allowing specification without a specifier undermines this proof.

Core Idea
Specification requires a specifier—will is that specifier.

The commentator presents a universal proof that divine will must exist because the act of specifying which possibilities come into existence requires a specifier. He then critiques the author's earlier allowance that a capable agent could specify without a specifier, arguing this closes the door to establishing will entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the two parts: the proof and the criticism.
The passage is concise—every word matters.
2
Identify the universal proof
Find the premise: from all possible things, some are brought into existence. This specification requires a specifier (al-mukhassis), which is identified as will (al-irada).
This is a standard kalam argument for divine will.
3
Understand the criticism
The commentator points out that the author (al-musannif) allowed that a capable agent could specify one of two options without a specifier. If that is allowed, then the proof fails because the specifier (will) is no longer necessary.
This criticism shows a logical tension within the author's system.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the commentator sees this as closing the door to establishing will absolutely. This highlights a fundamental disagreement about the relationship between capability and will.
Think about how each position affects the overall argument for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحجة التي تشمل الكل هي أن يقال تخصيص ما يخصص بالإيجاد من جميع المقدورات يحتاج إلى مخصص وهو الإرادة ، إلا أن المصنف لما جوز أن يخصص القادر أحد الطرفين من غير مخصص انسد عليه باب إثبات الإرادة مطلقا
And the proof that encompasses all is that it is said: The specification of what is specified for bringing into existence from among all possibilities requires a specifier, and that is will. However, when the author allowed that the capable one may specify one of the two sides without a specifier, the door to establishing will absolutely was closed to him.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents a universal proof of divine will. The proof states that among all possible acts, the fact that some are brought into existence while others are not requires a specifier (mukhassis), which is will. The commentator then criticizes the author: because the author earlier allowed that a capable agent could specify one option without a specifier, this undermines the proof and prevents establishing will altogether.

Key Terms
التخصيص
المخصص
الإرادة
المقدورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mere capacity (qudra) suffices for a specific act without needing will.
Correction: The commentator argues that specification requires an additional attribute—will—beyond mere capacity.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why did the author close the door to establishing will?
A) He denied divine will
B) He allowed specification without a specifier
C) He relied on scripture alone
D) He used a circular argument

Key Takeaway
The universal proof of will requires that specification demands a specifier, which is will; allowing specification without a specifier undermines the entire proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every specification of your existence—your birth, your circumstances, your very next breath—requires a specifier, who is that specifier? Have you lived today as if even the smallest details are willed by Allah? What would change if you fully acknowledged that?
?
The passage warns that allowing specification without a specifier closes the door to proving will. In your own life, have you ever attributed something to mere chance or 'just happening'? Repent from that and commit to seeing Allah's will in every specific event, starting now.
?
You have just learned that will is the necessary specifier. Does your heart truly submit to that will, especially in matters you dislike? Identify one area where you resist Allah's specification and resolve to embrace it as from His will.

Objection: Power Can Specify Without a Specifier
An objection that capacity can determine time without specifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that divine power could specify a time for action without an external specifier, and how it challenges the author's earlier position on free agency.

Core Idea
If a free agent can choose without a reason, why can't power specify without a specifier?

This passage presents an objection raised against the author's argument that will is needed as a specifier. The objector points to an apparent contradiction: the author earlier held that a free agent can give preponderance without a preponderator (murajjih). So why cannot divine power itself specify a time without a specifier? This objection forces the author to clarify the relationship between power, will, and specification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objector makes two claims: power can specify, and the author's view contradicts his earlier statement.
The objection is concise but dense.
2
Identify the two claims
First claim: divine power attaches to a time without a specifier. Second claim: the author's statement that 'the specifier is not power' contradicts his earlier position that a free agent can preponderate without a preponderator.
3
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector uses the author's own principle (free agent needs no preponderator) to argue that power should also be able to specify without a specifier. This is an argument by analogy.
The core of the objection is the inconsistency.
4
Consider a potential response
Think about how the author might defend his position. Could he argue that power is different from a free agent, or that specification requires will? The commentary will help.
This lesson only presents the objection; the answer may follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان لقائل أن يقول إن قدرته تعالى تتعلق بوقت للإيجاد دون وقت من غير مخصص وقوله : المخصص ليس القدرة . مناقض لما ذهب إليه فيما مر ، وهو أن المختار يمكنه الترجيح من غير مرجح
And one could say that His power attaches to a time for bringing into existence without a time from a specifier. And his statement: The specifier is not power. That contradicts what he held earlier, which is that the free agent can give preponderance without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is an objection raised against the author. The objector argues that divine power could attach to a specific time for creation without needing a specifier (like will). The author himself had said that the specifier is not power. The objector points out a contradiction: the author previously held that a free agent can give preponderance without a preponderator (murajjih), so by analogy, why cannot power specify without a specifier?

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's views on power and free agency are consistent.
Correction: The objector points out an inconsistency: the author allows specification without a specifier for a free agent but denies it for power.

Checkpoint
What is the contradiction the objector points out in the author's position?
A) The author allows specification without a specifier but denies it for power
B) The author says power cannot specify, but earlier said a free agent can choose without a preponderator
C) The author claims will is necessary but does not prove it
D) The author relies on scripture for will but not for power

Key Takeaway
This objection challenges the necessity of will by arguing that power could specify times on its own, drawing on the author's own principle of free agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face uncertainty about God's plan, do you ever act as if His power alone should be enough, ignoring the need to submit to His will? How does the objector's separation of power from will mirror your own impatience? What would change if you truly believed that God's will is the specifier of every moment?
?
The objector claims that a free agent can choose without any reason. Examine your own life: have you ever made a decision without any motive at all? If not, why do you assume God's actions are arbitrary? How does this lesson call you to trust that God's will is perfectly wise, even when you don't see the reason?
?
This objection reveals a tension between God's power and will. Do you live as though God's power is enough for your salvation without His guidance? Where in your life have you relied on your own ability rather than seeking His will? How will you realign your trust today?

Contradiction in Divine Knowledge and Necessity
Objection that divine knowledge also contradicts necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction between the principle that knowledge follows the known and the claim that God's knowledge necessitates events.

Core Idea
If knowledge follows the known, it cannot also necessitate it.

Imam Razi presents an objection against an opponent who argues that divine knowledge cannot be the specifier because it follows the known. However, the same opponent holds that whatever God knows will occur must necessarily occur. This reveals a contradiction: if knowledge is consequent upon the known, it cannot be the cause of its occurrence. Let's examine the objection closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it is a single objection structured around two conflicting claims.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage presents two claims from the opponent: (1) knowledge follows the known, and (2) what God knows will occur must occur. Write them down.
3
Understand why they contradict
Reflect on why these two claims cannot both be true. If knowledge is a consequence (follows the known), it cannot be the necessitator (cause) of the known. The objector highlights this inconsistency.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Consider how this objection fits into the larger discussion about divine attributes and the specifier. It weakens the opponent's position that knowledge is not the specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ولا العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم تناقض قوله: ما علم الله وقوعه يجب أن يقع لاستحالة كون الموجب تابعا لموجبه
And his statement: Nor knowledge, because it follows the known, contradicts his statement: What God knows will occur must occur, because it is impossible for the necessitator to be consequent upon its effect.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents another contradiction in the author's argument. The author said that knowledge cannot be the specifier because it follows the known (i.e., knowledge is consequent upon the object). But the author also holds that what God knows will occur must necessarily occur. The objector argues that if knowledge follows the known, then it cannot necessitate the known; rather the known determines knowledge. Thus there is a contradiction between saying knowledge is consequent and saying it necessitates.

Key Terms
العلم
المعلوم
الموجب
الموجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine knowledge can both follow the known and necessitate it without contradiction.
Correction: If knowledge is a consequence (follows the known), it cannot also be a cause (necessitator) because a consequence cannot precede or necessitate its cause. The objection shows this logical impossibility.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector see a contradiction in the author's statements about knowledge?
A) The author says knowledge is eternal but also temporal
B) The author says knowledge follows the known, but also says what God knows must occur
C) The author says knowledge is an attribute but also an act
D) The author says knowledge is a specifier but also not a specifier

Key Takeaway
The objection reveals that claiming knowledge is both consequent upon the known and necessitating it is logically inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Possibility Restricted to a Specific Time
Objection and answer regarding time-specific possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time-specific possibility does not serve as a valid objection against the need for a specifier, because absolute possibility (a necessary concomitant of essence) differs from restricted possibility.

Core Idea
Absolute possibility is essential; time-restricted possibility is not.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that possibility might be restricted to a particular time, which would remove the need for a specifier. He offers a two-part answer: first, the objection does not apply to actions outside time; second, he analyses the nature of possibility itself, distinguishing between absolute possibility (a necessary concomitant of essence) and restricted possibility (not a concomitant). This distinction is crucial for defending the necessity of a specifier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Pay attention to the objection and the two responses.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims that possibility (imkan) might be specific to a particular time, so no specifier is needed. Note how the author responds.
3
Understand the first part of the answer
The author says the objection does not apply to actions that do not occur in time. Think about why that is relevant.
Actions outside time include divine actions or abstract possibilities.
4
Examine the second part of the answer
The author argues that if possibility is time-specific, then the body cannot be described by it before that time, because the conditioned possibility does not exist earlier. Then he distinguishes absolute possibility from restricted possibility.
Absolute possibility is a necessary concomitant of essence; restricted possibility is not.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this distinction protects the argument that possibility requires a specifier. Why does the objection fail?
The objection confuses two different types of possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض بتجويز كون الإمكان خاصا بوقت معين لا يتوجه على الأفعال التي لا تقع في زمان . والجواب بأن الموصوف بإمكان الحركة هو الجسم يقتضي أن يكون الجسم هو الحاصل قبل ذلك الزمان وهو ليس بصحيح؛ لأن إمكان الحركة المشروطة بذلك الزمان لا يكون حاصلا قبل ذلك الزمان فكيف يكون الجسم موصوفا به وكون الإمكان من لوازم الماهية لا ينتقض بماذكره ، لأن الإمكان المطلق من لوازم الماهية والإمكان المقيد بشيء غير لازم لا يكون من لوازمها ولا يتناقضان باختلاف الدوام واللادوام لا يكون لاختلاف موضوعا
And the objection by allowing that possibility is specific to a particular time does not apply to actions that do not occur in time. And the answer that the described by the possibility of movement is the body implies that the body is existent before that time, and that is not correct; because the possibility of movement conditioned by that time is not existent before that time, so how can the body be described by it? And that possibility is among the necessary concomitants of essence is not invalidated by what he mentioned, because absolute possibility is among the necessary concomitants of essence, while possibility restricted by something non-necessary is not among its concomitants, and they are not contradictory by virtue of the difference between eternal and non-eternal, because of the difference of subject.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objector allows that possibility (imkan) could be specific to a particular time, so the need for a specifier is avoided. The author responds that this objection does not affect actions not occurring in time. Then he gives a detailed answer: if we say the body is described by the possibility of movement at a future time, that would require the body to exist before that time, which is not true because the possibility conditioned by that time does not exist before the time. Furthermore, absolute possibility is a necessary concomitant of essence, but restricted possibility is not. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الوقت
الموصوف
لازم الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility is always an attribute of an existing entity, and that time-specific possibility is still a property of the thing.
Correction: The author clarifies that time-specific possibility is not a necessary concomitant of the essence; it depends on external conditions. The body cannot be described by it before its time.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is a body not described by the possibility of movement before the time of movement?
A) The body does not exist before the time
B) Possibility is a necessary concomitant of essence
C) The possibility does not exist at all
D) The possibility is absolute

Key Takeaway
Absolute possibility is a necessary concomitant of essence, while time-restricted possibility is not; therefore, the objection that possibility could be time-specific does not undermine the need for a specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This lesson distinguishes between essential and conditional possibility. In your own life, what possibilities have you assumed are fixed that may actually be conditional? Will you re-examine them in light of Allah’s absolute attributes?

Time as a specifier leads to infinite regress
Refutation that time can specify possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why postulating time as a specifier for possibility leads to an infinite regress, and why this refutes the idea that time itself determines when an event occurs.

Core Idea
If time exists and needs specification, it generates an infinite chain of wills.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that possibility may be tied to a specific time. He argues that if time itself is not existent, it cannot have an effect, but if it is existent, it would require its own specification by another will and time, leading to an infinite regress. This reasoning shows that attributing specification to time does not avoid the need for a divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: if time is non-existent vs. existent.
2
Trace the regress
Follow the logic: if time exists, it needs another time and will, leading to tasalsul (infinite regress).
3
Connect to broader kalam
Understand that this argument is used to defend the necessity of a divine will as the ultimate specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله في الجواب عن تجويز كون الإمكان مقيدا بوقت : أن الوقت إن لم يكن موجودا استحال أن يكون له أثر ، وإن كان موجودا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول لأجل دليله على إثبات الإرادة، بأن يقال الوقت إن لم يكن موجودا استحال أن يتخصص بالإرادة وإن كان موجودا احتاج إلى وقت آخر وإرادة أخرى تخصصه به ويتسلسل . قوله : كون الماهية متقررة قبل وجودها بناء على أن الماهية متقررة حال عدمها فيه نظر ، لأن الماهية متقررة قبل وجودها وقبل عدمها قبلية بالذات ولا يلزم منه أن يكون تقررها حال عدمها إلا إذا كانت القبلية بالزمان . والقول بأن الحوادث مستندة إلى الاتصالات الفلكية إن أريد بالاستناد كون الاتصالات شرطا لوجوداتها لا ينافي كونها واقعة بقدرة الله تعالى
And his statement in answer to allowing that possibility is restricted by time: That if the time is not existent, it is impossible for it to have an effect, and if it is existent, the discussion about it is as in the first, because of his proof for establishing will, such that it is said: If time is not existent, it is impossible for it to be specified by will, and if it is existent, it needs another time and another will to specify it, leading to infinite regress. His statement: The essence (mahiyya) being determined before its existence, based on the view that the essence is determined in the state of its non-existence, is subject to scrutiny; because the essence is determined before its existence and before its non-existence by essential priority, and it does not follow from that that its determination is in the state of its non-existence unless the priority is temporal. And the statement that events are based on astral conjunctions, if by 'based' is meant that conjunctions are a condition for their existences, does not contradict that they occur by the power of Allah.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage contains several arguments. First, the author answers the objection that possibility may be time-bound by arguing about time itself: if time does not exist, it cannot have an effect; if it exists, then its own specification requires another time and will, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Second, he discusses the determination (taqarrur) of an essence (mahiyya) before its existence. The claim that the essence is determined in the state of non-existence is problematic because the priority is essential, not temporal. Third, he addresses the view that events depend on astral conjunctions, saying that if conjunctions are merely conditions, that does not contradict divine power.

Key Terms
الوقت
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that specifying time itself avoids the need for a divine will.
Correction: The argument shows that if time exists, it itself needs a specifier, leading to an infinite regress, thus affirming the necessity of an eternal will.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what happens if time is existent?
A) It can specify itself.
B) It needs another time and will.
C) It becomes eternal.
D) It has no effect.

Key Takeaway
The argument against time as a specifier shows that time, if existent, itself requires a will, leading to an infinite regress, thereby reinforcing the need for a divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if time itself justifies your delays or decisions, as if the mere passage of time is enough to make something happen, without turning to the One who alone has the will to specify all things?
?
When you rationalize your actions by saying 'the time wasn't right,' are you in reality avoiding acknowledging that every moment is contingent upon the will of Allah, and that your own will is dependent on His?
?
The argument shows that if time needed specification, an infinite regress follows. In your own life, do you rely on created things (like time) as ultimate explanations, rather than submitting to the fact that only Allah's will ends all regression?
